Chapter Eighty-Seven.
In the Extraction of the Placenta.
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, it is necessary to order the midwife to hold herself and then she should sneeze with a handkerchief and pull your hand on her mouth and nostrils. If it comes out this way, otherwise take a container.
And pierce a hole in the lid and place inside it herbs that open the mouth of the uterus, such as fennel, hyssop, dill, chamomile, wormwood, sage,  centaury and all these medicines or some of them, immerse them in water and put them on the fire. Then place a silver tube on the hole of the lid and the other end in the mouth of the uterus.
And hold it until the steam reaches the bottom of the uterus, then sneeze as we have mentioned, for the placenta will come out quickly. If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, order the midwife to dip her left hand in bear fat or  the saliva of a wild dog.
Then you insert it into the vulva and search with it for the placenta. If you find it, grasp it and pull it out little by little until it emerges. If it is attached deep in the uterus, insert your hand as we have described. When you find the placenta, pull it out little by little on the side of the womb.
Lest the uterus fall out due to strong pulling, it is necessary that you move it gently to the sides, right and left. Then increase the amount of pulling; then it responds and gets rid of its adhesion. If the opening of the womb is closed up, we have described the treatment by cauterization and treating the condition with herbs.
If it does not come out with all that we have described, do not resort to violence in re-pulling it. But rather tie what has come out of it to the opening of the womb to the womans thigh, then inject it with the fourfold ointment; for it softens her after days.
And it dissolves and comes out, except that if it becomes rotten, a bad smell rises from it to the stomach and head, which is harmful to the patient. Therefore, you should use the appropriate barley gruel.
Therefore, because some of the early practitioners experimented with fumigation with incense and dried figs, in the manner of the instrument that a woman uses for fumigating when she has menstrual obstruction or placenta retention, etc.
It is made of lead resembling a funnel, or it is made of copper, with the narrow end placed in the vulva and the wide end on the fire and incense made on embers. Hold it until that incense goes away and replace it with more.
Chapter Seventy-Seven
On the Treatment of a Non-Pierced Anus
A great number of boys are born with non-pierced anuses,  blocked by a thin membrane. It is necessary for the midwife to pierce that membrane with her finger, or else she should cut it with a sharp scalpel and be careful not to touch the sphincter muscle.
Then you place a cloth soaked in wine and oil on it, and then treat it with ointments until it heals. If you fear that it will be blocked, put a lead pipe into the opening for many days and remove it when the child wants to defecate.
And it may happen that the anus is obstructed also from a wound or tumor, so it is necessary to cut through that swelling and treat it with what we have mentioned of treatment and poultices.
The Eighty-Eighth Chapter.
In the Treatment of Fistulas That Occur Below.
Fistulas that occur below are a complication and thickening that occurs near the anus from outside or in the space from one of the sides, and the fistula is one or more.
And if that thickening opens and a whitish watery fluid or thin pus flows from it, and some of these fistulas may penetrate into the intestines and the peritoneum, and they may be non-penetrating. The penetrating ones can be identified by what comes out of them of feces and wind when using the bedpan.
And perhaps worms come out of it, and it may be fistulas if they are in the space penetrating into the bladder and the urethra, and it may be penetrating into the hip joint and the coccyx, and what is known as a fistula penetrating into the anus is also considered non-penetrating.
That you insert your index finger into the anus and insert a thin probe of copper or iron into the fistula from inside, if there is no projection in the fistula, if there is a projection then insert a fine lead probe or a hair from horse hairs.
Until you feel the probe or the hair in your finger, if you don't feel it at all and nothing comes out of the opening, such as feces or wind or worms as we have mentioned, then know that it is non-penetrating, so proceed to the treatment.
If the abscess is flowing into the bladder or the urethra, its sign is the urine coming out of it and preventing it from adhering to the place with medicines. But if it is flowing into the hip joint or the coccyx, its sign is the probe reaching there.
If it does not have protrusion to the bone and pain in the direction of it, and pus is always coming out of it, and there is no benefit from treatment or adherence with ointment, then all these discharging abscesses are never completely cured.
And its treatment is a futile effort for whoever suffers from it among ignorant physicians. But as for the one that is not discharging and not chronic, one hopes for its cure with iron as I describe and what has been tried, which is to lie the patient down in front of you on his back.
And the legs are moved up and the thighs are tilted toward his belly, then a probe of copper or lead is inserted if there is protrusion in the abscess until you know where it ends. If the patient feels it near the anus, then it is necessary to insert your index finger into the anus.
If you feel with your finger that the probe has entered itself exposed without feeling between your finger and it any bone or flesh, then at that time know for certain that it is discharging, so do not tire yourself in it, for there is no cure from it as we said.
And they said that it is cured in some people rarely, and from the treatment that one hopes for benefit, a thin iron is heated according to the size of the abscess as mentioned before, and it is inserted with a cover into the abscess until it reaches near the anus, then it is returned twice or three times.
Until you know that all those excess tissues that are intertwined have been burned, which resemble a feather tube, then treat it with a cloth soaked in butter until those burned tissues are extracted, then treat it with medicinal ointments if it is cured.
Otherwise, it does not heal without it from treatment ever. And if you introduce the probe and it does not reach your finger that is in the seat, and there was between it and the probe a thick veil of flesh or peritoneum, and you saw the nail on what is below the skin's surface, then cut the skin at the beginning of the nail. And you pass through the incision with the probe while it is in the nail until you reach where the tip of the probe ends and the probe comes loose and falls, then you clean those congealed meats that resemble a bird's feather tube, and nothing remains of it.
Then you treat it with the cohesive ointments until it heals, if blood overwhelms you and stands between you and doing your work to cut those meats, then the best thing you do and the fastest benefit is to cauterize it with fire or a sharp medicine because cauterizing with fire brings together two beneficial conditions: burning those excessive meats.
And cutting the blood and drying the humidity, then you treat it with the cloth moistened in butter or sulfur powder mixed with oil until the place forms pus and those burnt meats come out in pus, then treat the wound at that time with cohesive ointments for hardening the flesh.
And these are the medicines that have constriction and drying, so if it dries out, then it heals and there is no return of the ailment, if the nail has reached deep into the seat and away from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the seat and search with it.
If you feel the probe and between you and it a veil of peritoneum or flesh, and it was close to penetration, then there is no work done in it except by way of hoping and wishing that you use one of three ways: either you mold it as we have said...
or you cut it until you reach its bottom so that you can insert the cloth and treat from near, and do not overdo the incision lest you cut the muscle surrounding the seat, causing a condition where the patient experiences involuntary stooling, then treat it with what has been mentioned, and perhaps it heals as we have said.
And if you perforate that veil and pierce it after having split it near the anus as we have said, either with a probe or with another sharp instrument; then cleanse all the congealed flesh in the fissure to the extent you are able.
Then fill the wound entirely, together with the fissure and the upper opening until it is sealed and the opening remains open inside the anus so that it is easier for the patient, and the opening may be sewn in this way.
And if you insert the probe into the opening and it is on one side of the anus near the surface of the body with the skin and the ring of the anus, then take a probe with a perforated tip like a needle for sewing in this way:
And insert a loose thread of five threads or so into it, then insert the probe with the thread into the opening until it reaches its bottom; if it is inflated at the edge of the anus from inside near.
Then bring the thread out of that hole by inserting your finger into the anus and bringing out one end of the thread, and gather both ends together and tighten them, and leave it for a day or two; as long as the thread cuts in the flesh, tighten it well until those tissues between the ends of the threads cut off and fall away, then treat the wound until it heals and becomes sound.
And if the opening of the anus is not inflated, then open it as much as you can unless it is very deep; in this case, do not do so for the sake of the muscle lest you cut it; then make it as we have mentioned until it heals.
An image of the pointed scalpel used to split the anus, its edge is very sharp and the other side is not sharp lest it cut what is not needed as you see.
Chapter Eighty-One.
On hemorrhoids that bleed and their cutting and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids appear in two forms: either inside the anus, resembling red swellings or like grapes, and they may be small or large, with blood constantly flowing from them.
They may be singular or multiple, and they may appear outside the anus at its edges; however, those located outside the anus are usually less moist, with a constant flow of yellowish water or a small amount of thin blood, and they have the color of the body.
The treatment for those inside the anus is to instruct the patient to defecate and wash until the anus is exposed, revealing the hemorrhoids. Then, quickly grasp them with tweezers or your fingertip and cut them off at their base. If tweezers cannot be grasped due to moisture and relaxation.
Scrape it with a rough cloth or a piece of tanned bread, or pull it with your fingers then cut it and after cutting, place some sharp thorns on it so that they replace cautery, or apply cautery as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization.
Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals. If the anus does not yield to come out, inject the patient with a syringe containing a small amount of anesthetic to wash what is inside the anus and facilitate its coming out quickly when the patient defecates. As for external hemorrhoids, treating them is easy and simple.
This is done by grasping it with your finger or using tweezers to cut it, then treat it as mentioned above until it heals. As for those who dislike cutting with iron, one should use a needle of this type: take a loose thread and insert it into a needle.
Then, pull the hemorrhoid upwards and pierce it with the needle at its base from the other side. Twist the ends of the thread below the needle, which is crossing, and tighten the hemorrhoid tightly, then tie the thread and withdraw the needle. Repeat this for all the hemorrhoids and leave one untied.
Then, to cause the blood to drain from it, and place a cloth soaked in rose grease or sesame oil on the affected area, place it on the affected area and instruct the patient to remain still then leave him until it falls off.  If it has fallen off, treat the wound with ointments and all other treatments until it heals.
As for the treatment of a fissure, it often occurs from the dryness of the stool and obstruction of natural flow. If it persists despite treatment, it is necessary to detach it with a scalpel or your fingernail until it becomes moist and the outer layer that prevents it from healing is removed.
Then treat it until it heals properly. If it does not heal, cut it more deeply than before until it bleeds well and peels, then treat it as it will heal.
The Eighty-Second Chapter.
In the treatment of ingrown nails, both reversed and non-reversed, as well as dry calluses and blisters.
A nail is a rounded knot on the color of the body, resembling the head of a nail, and it can be found in all parts of the body, especially the soles of the feet and fingers. It causes pain when walking.
It is necessary to cut around the nail and grasp it with pliers or a wire and remove it from its root, then treat it. If you wish, cauterize it according to what has been mentioned in its chapter. As for dry calluses, one of them is a small, rough, rounded elevation above the surface of the body.
And it often occurs in the hands of young boys. Its treatment is to stretch the callus and cut it or remove it with a silk thread or hair until it falls off. If you wish, cauterize it as mentioned earlier with fire or a sharp medicine.
And ingrown and non-ingrown nails can be removed with the instrument mentioned in the treatment of a blister. As for a blister, it is also a small, rounded elevation, thick on the surface of the body, going deep. If it is affected by cold, you will feel a sensation similar to that of an ant bite.
It is in the whole body, and most of it is in the hands. Its treatment is to take a tube from an ostrich feather or a strong eagle feather and make that tube over the ant hill until the circle of the tube surrounds it from all sides, then turn your hand with the tube until you break what surrounds the ant hill.
And insert it into the depth of the flesh and remove the ant from its root, and if you wish, do this with a copper or iron tube in this manner.
The top of the tube to the wrist is solid and open so that it is easy for the fingers to grasp and open it, and if you wish, kill the ant as described before in the chapter on killing  the warts in the chapter on killing.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
In pictures of the instruments used for injections in diseases of the buttocks, diarrhea, and colic.
The syringe may be made of silver or porcelain or copper, hollowed out or hammered, and it can be made from this instrument small and large according to those who use it. And so the one used to treat small children, whose buttocks are narrow or painful, their syringes are very delicate. Picture of a large syringe:
The upper part that connects the tube is wide in this manner, it has a barrier where the tube is connected as you see, and its lower end which enters the buttocks is solid and very smooth to the wrist on one side, two holes, and on the other side, one hole.
The width of the hole is on the thickness of the needle or a little thicker, and the tube that puts the medicines in it is made from the bladder of an animal or from the tail of a sheep, shaped like a small tray, its diameter being an inch.
And it is pierced with many holes as it rotates, and the space between each hole is the thickness of a finger, then a thick thread from doubled yarn of ten threads or so is inserted into those holes and the tube is gathered together like a tray. When you have placed the medicine inside, hold tightly the head of the syringe between the stones with a firm and tight grip, then inject it. And this is an image of the circle of the incision and the hole.
And the way of inflation with it is that you fill the bladder or rectum with medicine while it is heated on a low fire, as much as possible for the patient's condition, and be careful not to make it too hot or too cold, then the patient lies on his back and raises his legs.
And a mat or sheet is placed under him, then the tip of the syringe is anointed with oil or egg white or linseed mucilage or the like, then it is gently inserted into the anus, then the assistant to the surgeon takes it in both hands and squeezes it forcefully until all that is in the syringe is emptied into the intestines.
Then the syringe is removed and the patient's legs are raised against the wall. If the medicine urges him to go, let him hold it as much as he can, and if he sleeps with it all night, it is more effective for constipation. If it was injected into diarrhea cases and its exit was hastened, then stop the injection until the diarrhea stops.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful tumors in the anus, let the syringe be very gentle and smoother than the syringes used for boys. And this is an image of a gentle syringe with its skin stretched over it.
Chapter Eighty-Four.
In the Treatment of Surgeries.
I mentioned in the division the treatment of things similar to burns, and I am here mentioning their treatment by hand and medicine fully. So I say that surgeries differ according to the thing being operated on and according to the location where the wound is.
The things that surgeries are performed on are many, such as a stone wound or a sword cut or knife wound or spear thrust or stick or arrow and similar to these are many things.
As for incisions according to the locations in the body, such as a wound that occurs on the head or neck or chest or abdomen or liver and similar organs.
And I describe the treatment of some incisions to make them a standard and law for all incisions, and I begin with simple head incisions, especially because the complex ones will be mentioned in the beginning of the third chapter of this book.
So I say that when a simple wound occurs in the head and there was no bone fracture, if it is from a stone or the like and only the skin was split and the wound was large and I feared for the patient the occurrence of hot swelling, then immediately  it should be drained using a cauterizer on the spot. And do not delay that and bring out from the blood according to its strength if nothing prevents bleeding, especially if the blood coming out of the wound is easy, let the draining be from the opposite side of the wounded area.
And warn the patient against fullness, drinking, and heavy foods, if something prevents bleeding, then he should reduce his food and avoid meat or drinks, and apply to the wound if there is a hot swelling cotton soaked in rose oil alone or with the drink that contains it in moderation.
And if the hot swelling is gone, apply to the wound if it is soft with its blood and has not been changed by the fresh air that I will describe to you shortly after, but if it has been changed by the air, then apply some ointments to it.
Until the pus is discharged and then treat it until it heals, if the wound is large and from a sword cut or the like and its edges have not come together with the surrounding tissue, collect them with stitching as I describe in stitching abdominal incisions.
If the skin was exposed to bone and adhered, but its hold was only in a slight place, then cut it off and then treat the wound with medicines that firm up its fleshy parts as a substitute for skin, and order the patient to eat foods containing strength like heads, tails, and the like.
If there is rot in the skin and it has not adhered to the bone, cut out all of the rotten flesh and throw it away then treat the wound. If in these wounds a vein or artery bleeds blood and the bleeding does not stop with medicines, then search for the vein.
If you hit it and it does not stop bleeding, cut it off with a scalpel and tie it up. And if necessity demands and what has been mentioned does not help, cauterize it until the bleeding stops. If these wounds are small, they are easy to manage; it is enough to treat them by putting this powder on them in their blood.
Before the air changes it, the recipe for the powder is to take from frankincense a part, and from mastic two parts, and from slaked or unslaked lime three parts.  All of these are crushed and sifted and stuffed into the wound and tightened well so that it adheres firmly with a good seal.
And it clots with blood, then you tighten it from above with bandages and leave it alone, do not remove it as long as it does not swell. For the medicine does not come off of it until it heals.  The lime itself can do this if you do not have frankincense and mastic.
And it can also do this with boiled rice flour in many large wounds if you are skilled at placing it on them and tightening them, and the wounds are fresh with blood as long as the air has not changed it. But if the air has changed it some, especially if it is in the summer time...
So there is no meaning in treating it with what has been mentioned by placing some prepared ointments on it, or placing a porridge made from barley flour with water and honey on it so that it draws out the pus, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
But if a fracture in the bone occurs with the wound and it is slight, draw it together with a splint. And I have mentioned the treatment of skull fractures in what comes after, in the surgery of the neck. If the wound is in the neck, there is no difference in treatment between it and a head wound if it is simple.
As for if a nerve from the neck or an artery has been cut, if it is a nerve, there is no remedy except to avoid treating it with anything that constricts tightly like the ginger root and saffron and similar things because they injure the nerve and tighten it.
And nothing cold should be placed on the wound at all because the essence of the nerve is cold and its connection to the brain which is the most noble of organs, and what you treat it with from medications should be soft like rose petals washed with fresh water several times moistened with oil or rose ointment and the mulberry.
If you also washed with fresh water and all minerals on this manner and the moist ointments, and as for if the wound is large, use stitching or closing its edges with bandages then treat it until it heals.
If the wound has a hollow and there is a hidden collection of pus at its bottom, drain it in the lowest place in it. If an artery has been cut in the wound and you have reached its bleeding blood, amputate it or tie it or cauterize it if necessity demands it.
If the wound has cut some or all of the pharyngeal pearls and the passages are intact, gather the edges of the skin with stitching on the windpipe and do not touch the windpipe but smooth it and return it to its natural shape then tighten it tightly and leave it for days.
Then treat it with ointments that have constriction and drying until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, open it up. If there are bone shards in it, extract all of them or as many as possible, and if you cannot remove them all, leave it until the wound softens and heals, making it easier to remove.
If the wound is fresh, apply the powdered herbs we described. If the bone in the wound becomes infected and festering, treat it with what will be mentioned in its chapter on surgery of the chest and between the shoulder blades.
If a wound has been made by a spear or knife and you see that there is an opening, examine it; if air comes out when the patient breathes, then know that it is a battle wound. If there is no opening and it is oozing blood, do not put any cauteries in it at first and do not tighten it lest the blood be trapped in its depths.
It may travel to the heart and kill the patient, but make a poultice of attractive honey and apply it twice during the day. If no ointment is available to you, put cotton wool in the mouth of the wound to absorb what comes out of it; and lay the patient down with their sleep on the wound.
Let it drain; if three days or more have passed and the patient shows no shivering, no rapid heartbeat, nor shortness of breath, and you see that all his other conditions are good, then know that the wound is sound; treat him with a bandage and all other remedies until it heals.
If it is difficult to see and the pus continues constantly, know that it has become festering; treat it accordingly. The patient should lie on their wound so that any liquid drains away; if the wound is in the chest or back, treat it as described above with stitching.
If it is large or cauterized, if it is small; if it has affected the bone and fragments of it are broken off, then search the wound and immediately remove those fragments. If they are clean, if not leave them.
Wash the wound because it will make it easier to remove them, but for all other wounds that occur in other parts of the body, their treatment is governed by what we have mentioned.
Chapter Fifty-Eight.
On Wounds in the Abdomen and the Protrusion and Suturing of Intestines.
The tear that occurs in the abdomen can be large, small, or medium; a large one can cause intestines to protrude, and their insertion and suturing are more difficult and challenging.
A small rupture may also be difficult to repair from another direction, so it is necessary that its return be more difficult. The intestines if not quickly inserted into their place after an hour will swell and thicken, making it difficult to insert them. For this reason, the middle-sized rupture is better because it does not make the return of the intestines as difficult as these two types.
Know that stitching the abdomen on four sides, the two common stitches are suitable for stitching the abdomen and all other surgeries of the body, and the other two specialized stitches are used for all surgeries, except that they are more specific to stitching the abdomen.
And I am describing them to you one by one with explanation and description, so I say that if the wound is small and something from the intestines has come out and it is difficult to return it, this is due to one of two reasons: either because of the smallness of the rupture as we said, or because the intestines have been affected by swelling from the cold air.
If that is the case, then it is necessary to warm it by dipping a sponge or cloth moistened in lukewarm water alone or perhaps cooked with cinnamon, saffron and ginger, and gently use it to treat the intestines until the swelling subsides.
And perhaps the drink containing ginger is better than water alone in dissolving the swelling, so when you see the swelling has dissolved, then lubricate the intestines with water that has been cooked with aniseed or cumin, because it makes it enter more easily.
If it becomes difficult to return after this treatment, then make a small incision in the rupture with the tool used for cutting pipes, and the first treatment if successful is better than the incision, but it is forced to be incised only when necessary and other methods fail. And here is a picture of the instrument:.
One side is concave and the other is not, and the thin end is not sharp like a scalpel but rather slightly blunt, it is a tool similar to a small olive as you see. If the wound widens and the intestines enter, then its return should be to its natural shape.
Its special location, if you can manage it, is best. But if the rupture is wide and in the lower abdomen, it is necessary for the patient to lie on his back and make his legs higher than his head. And if it is in the upper abdomen, then his head and chest should be higher than his lower body.
And also, if the rupture is on one of the sides of the abdomen, always make your intention and purpose directed towards the side where the surgery is, higher than the other side. Thus it should be used in large surgeries and in medium-sized ones.
But in small surgeries, place it as you are able. Then prepare a close assistant who holds the entire rupture with his hands and gathers its edges together, then reveals little by little to the one responsible for suturing.
And this is the characteristic of the general one stitch, which is to take a needle or several needles according to the size of the wound, then leave from the edge of the rupture the thickness of the thumb and insert one needle without putting thread in the edges of the skin. Along with the edges of the peritoneum which is under the skin from inside until it passes through that side, and you have gathered the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum together becoming four strands, then tighten with a doubled thread around the needle from both sides all together until the edges of the wound come together tightly.
Then leave the thickness of a finger as well, and insert another needle, then knot it with the thread as you did with the first needle, and keep doing that as much as you need with needles until you have filled all the wound. Let the needles be medium between thick and thin.
Because needles that are too thin easily cut through flesh, and those that are too thick are difficult to enter the skin, therefore it is necessary for them to be medium in thinness and thickness, and it should not be inserted into the edge of the skin very close.
So that the flesh will not be severed by a suture and the wound will not open before it has healed, nor shall it be drawn apart by stitching so that the wound may not unite, then the ends of the needles are cut so as not to harm the patient when he is sleeping and a support of rough linen is made on each side to hold the ends of the needles.
And it is left until you know that the wound has healed, and this type of stitching with needles is thus suitable for small wounds because it may suffice to stitch them with one or two needles or thereabouts.
Or the manner of stitching for a second time, commonly known, is that it gathers together with the stitching the four edges, meaning the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, in one go with a needle containing thread, moderately fine and not thick, then when this needle has finished passing through these four edges, it is returned from the side where it began itself.
So that the thread lies interwoven from above the wound, so that the stitching is like that of a sack which binds its contents and a thickness equal to the breadth of the little finger is made between each stitch and this stitching is known to all people.
And with this stitching, a wound that had been inflicted upon a man in his abdomen by a knife was sutured; the incision had been more than an inch and part of the intestines, about two inches from the middle intestine, had protruded; the rupture was in the middle of the abdomen.
He was placed after his intestines were kept outside the wound for twenty-four hours; the wound healed in about fifteen days and he was treated until he recovered and lived many years after that, carrying on with all of his affairs, and the physicians had declared him incurable.
And it is strange that I did not treat him with ointment because I was in a place where there were no medicines; so I would put cotton on the wound twice a day and pledge to wash it with honey water until he healed.
As for one type of special stitching, I mention it according to Galen's statement: It starts with stitching the skin and inserting the needle from outside to inside. And when the needle has reached into the skin and the muscle going in a straight line across the entire length of the abdomen...
I left the edge of the peritoneum on the side where I inserted the needle, and I inserted the needle into its other edge from inside to outside in the other margin. And when it reached that one, I inserted it again into this same margin from the outside to the inside.
And I left the edge of the peritoneum on this side, and inserted the needle into its other edge from inside to outside, and when inserting it in the peritoneum, I also inserted it into the margin of the muscle that is on its side until it reaches all the way through.
Then I also started from this side itself and lined it with the edge of the peritoneum on the other side, and I brought the needle out of the skin near it, then returned it into that skin and lined the edge of the peritoneum on the other side with this margin of the muscle.
And I brought it out of the skin on its side, and I did that one more time and again and again until I stitched the entire wound according to this pattern. The whole description of this stitch is that it stitches like a fur seam.
That it stitches the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin and leaves it once from the other side until it is finished. As for the second type of special stitching that Galen also mentioned, this is his statement verbatim.
That it stitches it according to the example of those who treat illnesses, as they collect each part to its counterpart, naturally; so they join the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge and the margin of the muscle to its other edge, and that is what I describe to you.
It is necessary to insert the needle into the margin of the omentum near you from outside and penetrate it into it alone, leaving the two margins of the peritoneum, then withdraw the needle and penetrate it from outside to inside in both margins of the peritoneum, then withdraw it again.
And penetrate it from inside to outside in the margin of the other omentum on the opposite side, and this type of suture is better than the common simple suture, which has been mentioned before, for this is the statement of Galen verbatim, and some have said that a suture can be straightened with two needles. This means that you insert a single thread into them and begin by inserting the needle from your side and penetrating it to the other side, then insert the other needle from the opposite side so that it penetrates from your side according to the suture of the seams.
And know that if the rupture is in the middle of the abdomen, its suture is more difficult than in other places of the abdomen. As for the treatment of the wound, its ruling in its treatment is the same as that of all other wounds, that is, if you grasp the wound fresh with its blood before the air changes it and retract the intestines, stitch them up, and secure them, then place a compress of hot dough on top of it.
If the wound has been changed by the air, then apply some of the ointments that you prepare until it scabs and the threads fall off and the peritoneum and omentum heal together, and then treat it like the treatment of all other wounds until it heals.
If you do not have medicines prepared, then apply clean cotton to it since it starts to scab and change it twice a day as I have taught you until it heals. In most cases, you do not need any other treatment if the wounds are simple, but if you fear that the wound will involve the vital organs in pain.
Then it is necessary to soak soft wool in lukewarm oil or rose butter and place it around the areas between the rib and armpit, if he feels pain or a foul smell in his intestines, which often happens, then inject him with a black, tepid astringent syrup.
And especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestines and become a penetrating wound to its cavity, know that what was from the intestines thick is easier to heal, and what was from them thin is harder to heal.
But as for the intestine known as the fasting one, it does not accept healing from a surgery that happens to it at all because of the multitude of vessels in it and their thickness, and the softness of its body and its proximity to the nature of the nerve, but if what emerged from the wound is the entrails and reached it fresh, return it according to your straightening of the intestine.
If a period has passed and it has turned green or black, then it is necessary to tie it with a thread above the place that darkened from it so that it does not expose bleeding, because there are vessels and arteries in the entrails, then cut what is below that bond.
And make the ends of the thread hanging from below the surgery outside it so that it is easy for you to pull it and remove it when the entrails fall and cauterize the wound,  mentioned the wound that occurs in the intestines, but if a tear occurred in the intestine and was small.
it can be healed in some people because I saw a man who had been stabbed in his abdomen with a spear and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, so I stitched the wound and it became a fistula that feces and air came out from it, so I treated him as I did not aspire to his healing. So I continued to treat him until he healed and the place united, when I saw that the place had united, I feared for the patient that something bad might happen to him in his belly, so he did not experience any misfortune at all and remained in the best of health. Truly he eats and drinks and has intercourse and goes to the bathhouse and takes care of himself, and some people who have experience have mentioned that when a wound occurs in the intestines and it is small, it should be sutured in this manner.
It is that the large ants' heads are taken and then the lips of the wound are brought together and an ant, with its mouth open, is placed on the lips of the wound. And when it seizes it and tightens its mouth, its head is cut off, and it sticks and does not separate, then another ant is placed near the first.
And this is continued with several ants according to the size of the wound, then it is returned and the wound is sewn, for these heads remain stuck in the intestine until the intestine heals and is cured and no harm whatsoever occurs to the patient, and it is also possible to sew the intestine with fine thread.
The thing which is pulled from the two points of the animal that sticks to it after it enters into a needle is that the end of this thread from the two points is taken and is pulled well, then a fine linen thread is tied at its end, and that thread is entered into the needle. And in it the thread of the two points is used to sew the intestine, and then it is returned to the abdomen, and this type of sewing with ants and two points is only by way of hope and desire.
As for if the rupture was large and wide, especially if it was in one of the thin intestines, there is no remedy and there is absolutely no cure for it.
The Sixth and Eighty Chapter.
In the Treatment of Colds and Nasal Discharges.
Know that every wound or tumor if it has aged and progressed and become an ulcer and did not heal and was constantly discharging pus, it is called in general, in any organ it was, a discharge and we call it a cold, and the discharge.
It is in truth a complicated, whitish, solid clot with no pain accompanying it, and it has a cavity like the hollow of a bird's feather.
And therefore, some called it a feather, and it is sometimes wet, constantly discharging pus, and perhaps the moisture stops at times, and this moisture may be much, or little, thick, or thin.
When speaking of wounds in the classification, I have stated that every sore which does not heal and in which flesh does not grow is due to one of nine causes: either from little blood in the body, or from its bad quality, or because there is firm flesh inside it and on its edges which prevents the growth of good flesh. Or because it is full of dirt and filth, or because the wound itself is foul and the material that supplies it is of bad quality, or because the medicine is not suitable for its treatment, or because there has been corruption in the region due to a pestilence, or because there is something special about the region.
Or because it is full of dirt and filth, or because the wound itself is foul and the material that supplies it is of bad quality, or because the medicine is not suitable for its treatment, or because there has been corruption in the region due to a pestilence, or because there is something special about the region. As in the city of Saragossa, where it is difficult for diseases to mature and tumors to heal, by nature, or because there is one bone or several bones there, and all these causes and their signs and treatments with medicines have been explained in the classification.
It is here necessary for me to inform you about the treatment of abscesses and colds which occur by way of manual work, know that abscesses can occur in all parts of the body, some of them ending at large bones, veins or nerves.
Or to the peritoneum or intestines or bladder or rib from the ribs or vertebra from the vertebrae of the back or joint from the joints that are constructed in large numbers like the hand and foot, and some of them lead near a main organ and so on.
If any of these fistulas are of this description, they are from the difficult diseases to heal and that do not accept treatment and working on them is effort and ignorance.  But if there are fistulas that have not reached these places mentioned and I have hoped and desired to treat them.
And I wanted you to reach that knowledge, so take a probe from copper or iron if the fistula passes on straightness, then probe it with it. If there is a winding in the fistula, probe it with a probe of thin lead because lead softens its body.
When entering it bends toward the winding, and if the fistula has many mouths that you cannot ascertain them with the probe, then inject one of its mouths with a syringe, because the moisture you inject into it flows towards the other mouths and seeps out.
Then inquire and examine in every way you can to know if there is bone or nerve, or was the fistula's opening far away or near, or was it a single fistula with many mouths, and stop at all of this with your ability from understanding the patient and his condition and the pain when you press on the place.
And like that from the signs and causes happening to the tumor, then if you stand on all of this with certainty, then at that time proceed to treatment with confidence and see if it is
The fistula is outwardly near or in a healthy place far from a joint or nerve or artery or vein or one of the places I mentioned to you, then cut open the fistula as previously described and remove what is in it of the pus and rotten flesh and excess meat.
And whatever grows in his mouth from flesh or a tumor and the like, treat it until it heals. If the abscess is deep and straight, then it is necessary to cut it deeply as much as you can and then cleanse it of all its rotten flesh.
Then use the softened cautery on the sharp medicines and insert it into the bottom of the abscess as far as you can reach with the iron, do this several times until that sharp medicine eats away all that remains in the bottom of the abscess from the rot.
Then close it with ointments that grow healthy flesh until it heals. If it does not heal by this,  then follow what was previously described. If the cause of the abscess is bone and you find this to be the case, then cut into it and split it as I have told you, unless something prevents you.
Sweat or nerve or a main organ as we said, if the bone was revealed to you and it had some rot and blackness, then clear it until its decay goes away, then treat it with what heals flesh until it heals. If it does not heal and the pus continues as it was.
Know that you have not reached all of the removal of the decay, so uncover all that is on it again and investigate its clearing and purification with the utmost effort then close it. If it heals, otherwise know that this decay is in a deep place from the decay, so uncover all that is on it again.
And investigate its clearing and purification with the utmost effort. If the decayed bone was a small bone and you were able to pull it out, then pull it out with the soft forceps that are suitable for this.
If there were many bones, investigate pulling them all out and do not leave any of them,  give your best effort. If something from the ligaments obstructed you below them and cutting them was not dangerous, then cut those ligaments and flesh if there was any and free the bones.
If you are unable to remove the bone at the time, place a covering over the wound and leave it for days until the flesh and ligaments around the bone rot. Keep the wound open so that the incision you made does not close.
But if you fear this, place upon it cotton soaked in powdered sulfur with oil or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments. For when these ligaments rot and the bones become loose, it will be easy to remove them and pull them out.
If it is a single large bone, such as the femur or tibia, and only its surface is affected, then remove it with a blunt instrument until the blackness and corruption disappear, then close the wound. If a large part of it is affected and the corruption has reached the marrow, then there is no choice but to expose it.
And cut it all the way until the corruption ends. Then treat it until it heals, and I tell you of a case that happened to a man in his leg to make it an example and aid for your treatment. This man was young.
Around thirty years of age, he was afflicted with pain in his leg due to a movement within his body until the materials reached his leg and caused a large swelling. There was no external cause, and then time passed along with the mistakes of physicians until the swelling burst open.
And much pus flowed from him, and he was badly treated until the leg became gangrenous and many wounds appeared, all of them oozing pus and bodily fluids.  A group of physicians treated him for about two years, but none of them were skilled in surgery.
Until they came to me, and I saw his leg, with pus flowing from those wounds in a large stream, and the man had grown weak and his complexion had turned yellow. So I inserted a probe into one of those wounds, and the probe reached the bone, then I cleaned all the wounds.
I found it to be  flowing into itself from all sides of the leg, so I cut and split on one of those openings until some of the bone was exposed, and I found it rotten, eaten away, black, putrid, and perforated until the hole went through to the brain. I then revealed what I had uncovered.
And I removed the rotten bone, and I think that there was no other corruption in the bone except for what I cut and revealed, and that I had removed it. Then I closed the wound with medicinal plasters for a longer period, but it did not heal together.  Then I returned and uncovered the bone again above the first uncovering.
I found the corruption connected to the bone, so I revealed what appeared to me also of that corruption. Then I removed my plastering and it did not close nor heal together. Then I uncovered it again and I kept cutting away parts of the bone until finally it healed, up to about a span of the bone being cut.
And I removed it with its brain, then I closed it with medicine and it healed quickly and was cured.  But this repetition in his work and splitting was due to the weakness of the patient and his inability to tolerate it, and my fear for him dying because he would always experience excessive vomiting and poor condition. He eventually recovered completely.
And flesh grew in the place of the bone, firm and solid, and his condition improved in his body, and his strength returned, and he acted normally in his affairs, and nothing hindered him while walking at all. If a bone had broken in a place in the body.
Then it should be exposed in this manner: taking a bandage and tightening it at the end of the broken bone, ordering him who stretches it upwards, then making another bandage from wool thicker than the first bandage.
Then it is tied onto the flesh underneath the bone, stretching its end so that the flesh is pulled downwards, and you uncover the flesh at the place you want to expose so that the saw does not harm the flesh, and you put a piece of wood or a plank under the bone from below tightly.
For if you do this, the saw will not refrain from cutting through the rotten bone, and it is necessary that the saw be placed slightly above the place of decay so that there may be no putrefaction within the cavity of the bone, so that it does not appear on its surface for the sense, then you would be compelled to widen it once more.
If the bone is rotten and not detached but some parts of it are connected to each other and the decay is in the middle or part of it, then expose the flesh from all sides of it completely, then place the wood from below, then saw it from the first side where the decay is.
And if you are finished with sawing, saw it from the other side, and let the sawing be a little distance from the decay as we have said. If the decay is in a joint, cut the decayed joint itself and saw the bone where it connects from the other side.
If the decay is in the connection of two joints, there is no cure except for amputation. If the decay is in the palm of the hand or the foot, the matter of treating it is very difficult, but you must peel away the decay as it appears to you and remove it and clean it as much as you can.
And by what means can you straighten it when it does not obstruct you with a vein or nerve, and know that the saws and knives for cutting these bones are many according to the position of the bones and their direction, thickness, flexibility and hardness, as well as their size, smallness, and whether they are brittle. Therefore, it is necessary to prepare for each type of work a tool suitable for that work.
And know that the works themselves may guide you to the type of tool you need if you have a long experience and knowledge of the arts of this craft, because knowing the nature of the craft and seeing cases of diseases is one of the skills of the craft. So he may deduce for himself what tools correspond to each disease, and I have depicted for you in the end of this chapter several tools.
