The seventy-eighth chapter.
In cutting limbs and separating bones.
The limbs may rot either due to an external cause or an internal one. If you treated that corruption with the treatment of medicines and it was not effective, and you see the corruption spreading in the limb and nothing stops it.
Then it is necessary to cut off that limb to where the corruption has reached so that the sick person may be saved from death or a great affliction which is greater than losing the limb, and a sign of one who has this is that the limb turns black until you think that fire has burned it.
If it decays after blackening until that decay spreads to what follows that limb and takes hold of the rest of the body, then act quickly by cutting it off, as well as if the cause of corruption is from the sting of some creatures like a sea scorpion or a snake or a viper and the like.
If the corruption or sting is in the tip of the finger, cut off the finger and do not delay for the corruption to spread to the hand, and if it has taken hold of the hand, cut it off from the base of the wrist and do not delay for the corruption to spread until it takes hold of the forearm.
And if it takes hold of the arm, cut off the arm at the elbow joint itself. If the corruption progresses and you see it taking hold toward the shoulder, do not cut off the shoulder at all, for in that there is the death of the patient, and use other treatment to the best of your ability.
And do likewise with the leg if the corruption takes hold in the toe, cut off the toe at one of the joints, and if it takes hold in the foot, cut off the whole leg. If it rises to the knee, cut off the leg at the knee joint.
And if the corruption has reached above the knee, there is no remedy but to leave it and consign the patient to death.  The method for cutting off a limb or severing it is to tie a ligature below the place you want to cut, and tie another ligature above the place.
And the servant extends one ligature down and another servant extends the upper ligature up, and you strip the flesh between the ligatures with a wide scalpel until all the flesh is exposed, then it is cut or severed, and it is necessary to place linen cloth from all sides.
So that the saw does not touch the correct place and expose the patient to added pain and a hot swelling, if bleeding occurs during your work, stop the bleeding quickly or place some pointed pieces of wood on it to stop the blood, then return to your treatment until you are finished.
Then you tie the injured limb with a bandage that heals and mends it until it heals, and I tell you an example of a case of a man in his leg this very condition that I describe to you, namely, that a blackness occurred in his leg with burning like fire.
And this rot was the first to happen in his finger until it took hold of the whole leg, so the man immediately severed his leg from himself when he saw the rot spreading in the limb with the intense pain and burning, cutting it off at the joint, and he healed.
And when a long time had passed, that same rot appeared to him in his right index finger, so he came to me. I treated it with the remedies that I brought for his hand after cleaning his body. But the rot did not recede and kept spreading in the other finger.
Until the rot took hold of his hand, he called me to cut off his hand. I refused him out of hope that the rot would recede, and I also feared for him at the cutting off of his hand, death because the strength of the man was on the verge of collapse. When he despaired of me, he left for his country.
And news reached me about him that he severed his whole hand and healed. But I narrated this story so that it would be a help for what happens of this kind of disease and to be a guide by which one can deduce and act upon it.
The Eighth-Eighth Chapter.
On the Treatment of Abscesses and How to Inject Them with Remedies.
If a swelling occurs in some of the fleshy organs and its duration prolongs until it gathers for a period and then bursts or ruptures, expelling all that was inside it from the pus, and the place remains empty like a vessel.
And the skin on it, like a sheet, has become slack and was not severe in its corruption that it affected bone or nerve or ligament. And from here it deserves to be called an abscess, and it is not called a carbuncle except if its duration prolongs until the corruption affects any of these organs.
When this is called a fistula or a suppurating ulcer, its treatment is to cut all of that skin as it rotates and especially if it has become flaccid and like parchment and you are certain that it does not adhere to the site due to its corruption.
If you hope that the skin will adhere and it has not reached that degree of corruption and there is a thickness of flesh in the skin, its treatment is with the injection, which is to look at the hiding place; if it is large and the pus that extends from it is foul-smelling, inject it with the Egyptian medicine.
Which is honey and oil and wax and ginger, equal parts gathered in a vessel and cooked on the fire until the medicine thickens and comes to the thickness of honey, then take from it what you need and soften it with water and honey and inject it into the hiding place and tighten its mouth and leave the medicine in it for about two hours.
Then you remove it in the afternoon and do that for days until the hiding place is cleansed and the stench goes away, and he may inject with ash water if this medicine is not prepared for him, which is to take the ash of acacia wood or oak wood and pour water on it.
Then filter it and inject with it until you are certain that the hiding place has been rinsed, if there is no possibility for the patient on the Egyptian medicine or ash water, then inject him with water and honey mixed with something of ground ginger.
Or inject him with honey and syrup mixed because it is the nature of honey to cleanse and rinse, and the syrup adheres to the hiding place, especially if the syrup has a good amount of firmness and dryness; if you do this several times and are certain that the corruption of the hiding place has gone, then inject it with what promotes the growth of flesh in it.
Such as taking from the palm ointment and dissolving it with rose oil and astringent syrup, or injecting him with some of the medicines and ointments that we have proven their qualities in the treatise on ointments; if the mouth of the hiding place is narrow and does not contain the syringe tube.
Slightly widen it with iron or place a tightly woven cloth soaked in Egyptian ointment or the ointment of serpent root until it widens. And if the mouth is also wide, stitch its lips together and leave an opening that allows for the insertion of the syringe without excess.
And if the mouth from which pus flows is raised upwards, cut it in its lowest place so that the pus flows downwards because if the pus is retained in the depths of the hidden place, it prevents the flesh from growing there.
But if you cannot cut the hidden place downwards as you desire, then form a protrusion of the member from which pus flows easily according to what seems appropriate to you and make sure nothing of the pus is retained at all.
As for the ointments that are applied to the hidden place from the outside and how they are placed on it, take one of the suitable ointments matching what you desire and spread it on a linen cloth and let the cloth be the size that covers the entire hidden place. Then cut a hole slightly wider than the mouth of the hidden place with scissors and spread some soft ointment on another cloth, the size of the hole, and place it on top, keeping a distance from the mouth of the hidden place so that the pus can easily exit from it and do not remove the large cloth until after.
For several days, and as for the one on the mouth of the hidden place, it is the one that you remove at every time so that what has accumulated there of pus exits and to know what flows from it of pus whether it is much or little, ripe or unripe,.
And with this also, inspect the location of the hidden place itself whether the owner feels pain there or not and whether it has a swelling or not. If you continue treatment like this and see the material that exits as light on the mouth of the hidden place.
Know that if the hidden place has adhered or is close to adhering, then treat it after a day or on the third day.  And when you have treated the wound, replace the opening with another and renew the ointment. And if pus flows from the hidden place after a long time, thin in texture, do not despair of healing.
Then heal it is close because some people delay the growth of flesh in their wounds and others are the opposite, and if pus flows from the hidden place after many days unripe, know that the hidden place has not adhered,  and if the adherence of the hidden place is delayed.
And if it is prolonged, increase in the drying of the medicines and let them be medicines that are in their texture moist and in their power dry like the palm ointment if it has been increased with an excess of the addition of the calamint. And one of the most effective things to treat it is to take the palm ointment.
And it is treated with rose oil and sprinkled with old syrup of moderate strength, then it is covered with it and used. And he may do this if the palm ointment is not available to him. Honey when it is cooked until it is thick and used or take from saffron and patience and musk and grind them all together and sprinkle them on honey while it is on fire. Then spread it on a cloth and tie it to the hidden place or spread it with honey that has been cooked until it thickens and sprinkle the medicines on it and place the cloth on top of it and tie it.
And it may be used in this case the long turmeric and the root of the asphodel, and the powdered caraway, and chamomile singly or together ground and sifted and sprinkled on the honey that was described and used. If the hidden place is prolonged and does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption in its depths.
And it has been effective in bones or in all other hard tissues or ligaments, the treatment of the poultice as previously mentioned.
Chapter Ninety-Eight
On the treatment of ingrown toenails and diseased nails, and on amputating an extra finger and separating fused fingers.
An ingrown toenail is fleshy tissue that grows under the nail of the thumb or foot, and it may grow in all fingers; if left untreated for a long time, it swells and becomes hot and suppurating.
or it may rot and fester until it eats the nail's root, and perhaps ruins the entire nail, and possibly the decay reaches the bone, causing a foul smell, and making the fingertip wide and its color becoming like that of pus. If you treat it with what we mentioned in this division, but your treatment does not work.
Then it is necessary to cut with a knife all the excess tissue that grows from under the nail, and then cauterize the wound afterward, for cauterization in this case is very beneficial. But if the bone is healthy and the nail is also healthy.
And if the corner of the nail has driven flesh inwards, causing it to be pierced and harmed, then you should place a thin reed under the corner of the nail that is piercing the flesh and raise it upwards, and cut that flesh gently.
And put on the remaining flesh of the cauterizing medications until all of it goes away, then treat it with ointments until it heals. But if the decay has affected the bone, then you should cut that bone and remove it because it does not heal as long as there is decayed bone at all.
If you see the decay and infection moving in the finger, then cut it at one of the joints as mentioned above, then treat it until it heals. If the nail is struck or bruised and causes severe pain, it is necessary to first cauterize the wound.
Then you split the nail with a sharp scalpel, an oblique incision from top to bottom, and be careful not to reach with the incision to the flesh under the nail because doing so will cause severe pain to the wound and be the cause for excess tissue growth in that place, then treat the place with what relieves pain.
As for the extra finger that is born in some people's hands, it may be all flesh and may have bone in some parts and there may be a nail, and its growth may be from the base of one of the fingers joints.
Or its growth may be from some of the finger joints, and those that grow in some joints of the fingers do not move, and those that grow at the joint of the finger may move, so if it is only flesh, it is easy to cut it off, by cutting it off at its base with a broad scalpel.
As for the one whose growth is at the base of the joint, its treatment is difficult, so it is better to avoid cutting it. As for the one that grows in the finger at one of the joints, it should first be cut off, flesh being cut circularly to the bone, then the bone is spread with one of those saws suitable for it.
Then it is treated until it heals. As for the adhesion that occurs to some fingers, this often occurs and may be what a person is born with or due to the closing of a wound or a burn, etc., so it is necessary to cut open that adhesion.
Until the fingers return to their natural shape, then a piece of felt or cloth soaked in rose oil is placed between them so that they do not quickly adhere again and are separated from each other, or a thin lead sheet is placed between them until the place heals properly.
And similarly, if adhesion occurs to some fingers on the hand, that adhesion is cut open according to what appears appropriate and repairs the shape of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
On Cutting the Ligaments.
Ligaments are twisted, thick veins filled with dark-colored sludge, and they occur in most parts of the body and more commonly in the legs, especially in the buttock, thighs, and those who carry heavy loads. It is necessary first to use a poultice from black cumin, strong multiple times.
Then the surgeon will open the abscess, and as for its treatment with iron, there are two kinds: one is to make an incision and remove the black blood, and the other is to drain the sweat and remove it entirely.  As for making an incision, this is how the leg is treated first with hot water, yes indeed.
Until the thick, dark blood dissolves, then the leg of the patient is bandaged from above his hip to below his knee with a cloth, and then the sweat is incised in one place or two places or three wide incisions.
Then you drain the black blood with your hand from below the leg to above and from above to below until it comes out in a quantity that you deem sufficient or less than the strength of the patient's resistance, then tie it off and order him to avoid foods that produce black bile, and this is repeated, the evacuation and incision.
When the veins are full and that harms the patient, as for draining it, it is done in this way: the leg of the patient is shaved if there is much hair on it, then he is made to bathe and his leg is treated with hot water until it reddens and sweats or feels a strong exercise.
If he does not have access to a bath so that the limb is warmed, then the skin is incised opposite the sweat duct, an incision lengthwise either at its end at the knee or below it at the heel, and then the skin is opened with tweezers and the sweat is peeled away from all sides until it becomes palpable.
And when it appears, you see it red and glistening; and when it is completely freed from the skin, you also see it as if it were a tendon. Then, something (is inserted) under it until it rises and comes out of the skin, and it is suspended with a blind, smooth pin, then another incision is made near that incision, about three finger widths.
Then the skin is peeled away from the sweat duct until it appears, then it is raised with the instrument as you did before and suspended with another pin as you did first, then another incision is made or several incisions if you need that.
Then pull it and cut it at the end of the incision near the heel, then pull it and cut it until it comes out of the second incision, then pull it to the incision above it and do this until you pull it from the third incision, the highest of all incisions, until when it all comes out, cut it.
If it does not respond to pulling and cutting, insert a needle with a strong doubled thread and tie it, then pull it, and insert the instrument under it and feel with your hand in every direction until it comes out, and be careful that it does not break. If it breaks, it will be very difficult for you to pull it.
And it is inserted into the patient from this [incision], so if you have pulled out all of it, place on the location of the incisions wool soaked in syrup and rose oil or ointment, and treat it until it heals. If the appendix is swollen with folds and twists in directions.
And if it was not straight as we mentioned, then it is necessary to cut it on each side of its folds and places of twisting, then tie it with sutures until you pull it all out, and be careful when cutting it that you do not cut the vein or injure it because it will be difficult for you to pull it, so be cautious in your effort.
Image of the instrument used to make an incision into the appendix:
Image of the blind suture:
It does not have a point like the other sutures, and it is not sharp at the tip so that it will not injure the vein, and it is thick in its bending and smooth because if it was thin, it would cut the vein with its sharpness. It should rather be as thick as we mentioned.
Chapter Eighty-Nine.
On removing the hernia of the inguinal canal:
This hernia is produced in the legs in hot countries like Hijaz and Arabian lands, and in hot regions with little cultivation, and it may also be produced in other places on the body besides the legs, and its production is due to a swelling that occurs under the skin.
As for the worms within living bodies and maggots, it is necessary to treat them; those that are born between the skin and flesh, and the sign of their beginning is when inflammation occurs in the leg, then the place becomes blistered and then pus begins to flow from that place.
As if it were the root of a plant or animal, and when its tip appears, it is necessary to wrap it around a small piece of lead whose weight is from one dirham to two dirhams, bind it and leave the lead hanging from the leg.
And every time something emerges from it to the outside, wrap it in lead and bind it; and if it becomes long, cut some of it and wrap the rest, and do not cut it from its root before all of it comes out, because if you cut it, it will contract and enter into the flesh, causing a suppurating boil and fistula in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore, it is necessary to manage and draw it slowly until it comes out from the end and nothing remains of it in the body; and this discharge may emerge from some people with a length of five cubits or ten.
And it has reached me that it emerged from a man for twenty cubits, and if it were to be cut off while treating him, insert a probe into its hole and pack it with a long bandage together with the body until everything in it is emptied, and try to heal the place with medicine for days.
Then treat him with the treatment of tumors; and this discharge can be multi-branched, especially if it appears in the joint of the leg or in the leg itself, causing many openings and emerging from each opening a branch; its treatment is as mentioned in the division and what has come before.
The Twenty-ninth Chapter.
In the wound on the maggot born under the skin and is called the cattle disease.
This disease is called in some countries, among us, cattle disease because it often affects cattle; it is a small worm that is born between the skin and flesh and burrows throughout the body, ascending and descending.
It becomes evident to the touch when it is moved from one part of the body to another until it breaks through where it has broken through the skin and exits, forming a kind of pus from some of the humors as seen in worms and lice and the love of gourd within the abdomen.
And what is expected from those who are harmed by it, if it travels in the body and rises to the head and reaches the eye, perhaps it will open there and exit, blinding the eye, and this happens often. So if you want to treat it and remove it, then that is done when it is touched and felt.
It is necessary to tie above and below it with a strong bandage, then make an incision on it and remove it. If it sinks into the flesh and you don't find it, apply heat to the area with fire until it burns. The greatest thing that is expected is its damage to the eye as we mentioned.
If you see it has become in the head near the eye, tie tightly under it on the forehead, then make an incision and remove it. It is necessary for the patient to be treated with medications that cleanse the body of bad humors and avoid foods that produce pus.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On the Incision for the Disease Known as Nafrah.
This disease which is called Nafrah in our country is a pain that occurs in some organs and then moves from one organ to another, and I have seen it as I describe to you.
I was called to a sick woman in some of the desert, and she showed me a slight swelling in the armpit of her arm. After an hour, I saw that swelling moving with the tendon as a worm moves, rising towards her shoulder at the fastest speed possible, like mercury.
As it moved from place to place, the pain disappeared from that spot and settled in her shoulder. Then I sat for an hour and it flowed through her entire body until it was in her other arm, and she told me that it circled her whole body as I had seen.
I was amazed by the speed of its transition from one member to another, and I had not before seen this disease with my own eyes in this way. Except that I saw a group who found pain moving from member to member, but I did not see it with my own eyes as I saw it in this woman.
I did not appreciate this except that the woman was from the desert dwellers, dry-bodied and with exposed veins, so from here the sense appeared of the wind moving, and it is necessary that it does not appear on this measure in people of affluence and soft bodies with hidden veins.
So if you want to treat it and the sufferer feels pain, and it appears before you as we mentioned, then pull above it and below it with haste and cut on it until that trapped wind exits and the place is emptied.
If you do not see it with your own eyes, treat it by dusting the body and what expels wind and spreads it like the love of mint and the love of spearmint and similar medicines.
Chapter forty-nine.
On extracting arrows.
Arrows differ according to their types and according to the places where they are located in the body. As for their difference according to types, some are large and some are small, some are hollow glass and some are solid glass, and some have three angles and some have four angles.
And some have tongues and some have fragments. As for those that are according to the organs where they are located, they are of two types: either the organs are from main cavities like the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like. So when an arrow is located in one of these organs and the signs of death that I describe to you appear, then it is necessary to avoid extracting that arrow from it, for death will befall its owner in most cases. And when these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow is not hidden deep in the organ, then extract it and treat the wound. And from the signs of the brain if an arrow strikes it and pierces the bone and wounds the membrane on the brain, then it exposes severe headache, dizziness, and vertigo.
And when these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow is not hidden deep in the organ, then extract it and treat the wound. And from the signs of the brain if an arrow strikes it and pierces the bone and wounds the membrane on the brain, then it exposes severe headache, dizziness, and vertigo. Redness in the eyes, redness of the tongue, thirst, confusion, and a feeling as if something is being thrown within the head. And perhaps blood comes from the nostrils or ears, and perhaps speech ceases and the voice disappears.  From the wounds location white fluid similar to porridge flows, like that of meat juice.
If these signs appear to you, refrain from treating the patient and removing the arrow if it has not yet been extracted. But as for the signs of an arrow that has struck the heart, and was near the left breast, and he feels as if it is lodged in a solid thing, not a hollow one.
And perhaps the arrow has a movement similar to the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, followed by coldness of the extremities, cold sweat, and pallor. Know then that death is inevitable. And the sign of an arrow if it has wounded the lung is the exit of frothy blood from the wound.
And the vessels that follow the neck swell and the color of the patient changes, and he breathes a high breath and requests to inhale cold air. If the arrow has struck the diaphragm in the chest, it is near the small ribs and breathing is great with severe pain.
And he coughs and all the organs of his groin move. If the arrow has struck the liver, severe pain follows, and blood similar to the liver in its redness exits from the wound. And if the arrow has struck the stomach, perhaps something undigested exits from the wound.
And it is clear if the arrow has struck the abdomen and it has swollen, and something of feces exits from the wound or the intestines or a ruptured bowel, there is no hope for treatment or extracting the arrow. If the arrow has struck the bladder and urine exits.
And a part has protruded from it and the pain intensifies on the patient, know that he is doomed. But as for the rest of the organs like the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, lower back, clavicle, thigh, and leg and similar organs, most likely they will be unharmed.
When an arrow does not strike a vein or nerve and is not poisoned, and I tell you some of what I have seen of the matter of these arrows so that you may infer from it your treatment, namely an arrow which had fallen upon a man in his eye socket at the root of the nose.
And I extracted it for him from the other side under the lobe of the ear and he was cured and nothing ill befell his eye, and I extracted another arrow for a Jew who had fallen upon the lobe of his eye under the lower eyelid and the arrow had been hidden and I did not inflict on him except its small tip which adheres to the wood.
And it was a large arrow from the arrows of the Qas'i, composed and square of iron, smooth, which had no ears, so the Jew was cured and nothing ill befell his eye, and I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian and the arrow was Arabic.
Which one has ears, it split on him between the tonsils and had gone deep into his throat so I labored with it until I extracted it and the Christian was safe and cured, and I extracted another arrow for a man who had fallen upon his belly and we estimated that he would die from it.
For when he remained for thirty days or so and nothing changed in his condition, I split on the arrow and worked on it until I extracted it and he was cured and nothing ill befell him, and I saw a man who had fallen an arrow in his back so that the wound closed over it.
For when he was after seven years, the arrow came out in the root of his hip, and I saw a woman who had fallen upon her belly an arrow and the wound closed over it and the arrow remained and nothing changed in her condition, nothing did she find harm from him in anything of her natural activities,.
And I saw another man who had fallen an arrow in his face and the wound closed over it and he remained finding no much pain from it, and like this many, and I extracted an arrow for a man from the Sultan's cavalry who had fallen upon the middle of his nose and it leaned to the right side slightly.
The arrow was entirely missing and I was summoned to treat it after the arrow had entered for three days. I found the wound from the arrow to be very narrow, so I explored it with a fine probe and did not feel anything. However, he felt tingling and pain behind his ear from the right incision.
I suspected that the tingling was from the tip of the arrow, so I covered the area with a strong adhesive bandage and it ripened. I hoped that the area would swell and show a sign of the arrow, so I cut into it, but nothing happened in the area to indicate that the arrow had reached it.
So I continued with the bandage on him for many days, and nothing happened. It closed up in that time, and the patient remained unable to get it out until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and told me.  I then placed a strong pungent medicine on the wound for many days.
Until it opened up and I probed it, and I felt the tip of the thin arrow sticking in the wood. Then I increased the opening of the wound with that strong medicine until we saw the tip of the arrow with our own eyes. It took me about four months with him.
Then when the wound widened and I was able to enter the forceps into it, I pulled and moved it, but it didn't respond to coming out. So I kept soothing him and working on him with various tools until one day I grasped it with firm forceps, as you see its image at the end of the book.
And the doctors ruled that the cartilage in his nose couldn't be sutured, so I closed it and the wound healed and the patient recovered completely, not being bothered by anything at all. And I tell you how I removed some arrows to make this a measure and a guide for what I haven't mentioned.
Because the parts of this craft and its details are not grasped by description nor encompassed by a book, but rather the skillful artisan measures the little against the much and from what has been present to what is absent and derives a new work and a new tool in the case of strange occurrences.
If you descend from this craft, I say that the arrows exit from the organs in which they have lodged in two types: either by drawing from the place where they entered or from the opposite side, and those that exit from where they entered.
Or that the arrow is prominent in a place of inflammation, so it is drawn and removed. If it does not yield to you for exit from its time of entry, then it is necessary for you to leave it for days until the flesh around it decays so that it is easier to draw and remove it. Similarly, if lodged in bone.
And if it does not yield to you for exit, leave it also for days and repeat drawing and movement each day because it will exit.  If it does not yield to you for exit after days, then it is necessary that you drill around the arrow in the same bone from every side with a fine drill until it widens for the arrow, then draw and remove it.
If the arrow is lodged in a bone of the head and has penetrated into one of the cavities of the brain and some of those symptoms that I have mentioned to you have appeared from the patient, then hold back from drawing the arrow and leave it until his condition clarifies after days.
Then proceed in drawing the arrow and removing it. If it is deeply lodged and does not yield to you for drawing, then use the drills around the arrow as I have described to you, then treat the place until it heals. But if the arrow has been hidden in a place of the body And if it is not felt, then search for it with the probe; and if you feel it, draw it out with some instrument suitable for drawing it out; but if you are unable to do so because of the narrowness of the wound and the depth of the arrow's entry, and there was no bone or nerve or vessel there.
Then make an incision over it until the wound is widened and you are able to grasp the arrow so as to withdraw it; if it has barbs, grasp them and free the flesh adhering to them from every side with all the skill you can; if you are unable to free the flesh in the grip of the barbs, then cut through them and open them until you are freed.
And if you try to withdraw the arrow from any place, use your hand with gentle motions in all directions until you free it; and be as gentle as possible so that the arrow does not break and make it difficult for you to draw it out; and if you are unable to do so within a reasonable time.
Leave it for days until the flesh around it decays, then attempt again; for it will be easier then; and if you are faced with bleeding, use what was mentioned in its chapter; and take care not to cut any vessel or nerve or tendon, and use your skill.
In every way you can free the arrow, and let it be done gently and carefully, and stabilize as described to you; and it is fitting for you that when you draw out the arrow, the patient should be in the position he was in at the time of the arrow's impact, for this is more suitable.
If you are unable to do so, use whatever shapes you can; as for the arrow that exits from the opposite side, either something may have protruded from it externally, or you will feel the tip of the arrow with your hand near the top of the skin, and see that it is emerging.
Then make an incision over it, and let the incision be as wide as your fingers can reach; then draw it out; for it will be easy to exit; if it is stuck in bone, then use your hand with turning motions until the arrow impinges on the bone and widens itself, then draw it.
Otherwise, leave it for days until it comes out, if the shaft of the arrow is in it, push it out with it. If the shaft has fallen out and you want to use pushing, insert the grooved tool into it so that its cavity enters the wound of the arrow then push it with it.
If the arrow is hollow, push it out with a tool that enters that cavity, for the arrow will come out easily thereby. If the arrow is poisoned, it is necessary that you excise all the flesh which has become poisoned by it if you are able to do so then treat it with what is suitable for it.
If the arrow is in the chest or the abdomen or the bladder or the flank and was close to what can be felt with the probe and you were able to make a cut on it, make a cut and be careful not to cut a vein or nerve and take it out.
Then suture the wound if it needs suturing then treat it until it heals, image of the callipers used to attract the arrows:
Its ends are like the beak of a bird, made as if it were a cold iron when it grasps an arrow or something, and she does not let it go.  And they can make kinds of them large and small and medium each according to the size of the arrow, its smallness, the wideness of the wound and its narrowness.

Image of the solid pusher:

Chapter Fifty-Nine.
On Phlebotomy
The veins which it is customary to bleed in the body are thirty veins, of them six in the head, the two pulsating veins behind the ears known as the  "hissers," and the two veins in the temples that appear on the outside, and the two veins in the sockets of the eyes known as "the seers."
And the sweat that stands upright in the middle of the forehead and the sweat that is in the tip of the nose and the glands that are in the neck and the two sweat glands that are on the upper lip of the mouth and the two sweat glands that are on the lower lip, and these veins known as the jeharak and the two veins under the tongue.
As for the veins that are cut in the arm and hand, there are five veins: one is the qifal, which is on the outer side, and it is called by the common people the vein of the head, and the akhal, which is the middle vein, and it is composed of a branch of the baslik and a branch of the qifal, and it is called by the common people the vein of the body.
And the baslik, which is located on the inner side, and it is also called the armpit, and it is called by the common people the vein of the abdomen, and the cord of the arm, which is located on the forearm, and this is where it attaches to, and this is what appears above the thumb clearly, and the asilim.
And this is the vein between the ring finger and the little finger, it has two branches, and in the leg and foot there are three veins: one under the kneecap on the outer side, the second is the safen, and its location is at the heel on the inner side, and the vein of the gluteus maximus, and its location is at the back of the heel on the outer side.
And in the other leg there are three veins like it, as for the two veins behind the ears, their benefit is cutting them for chronic colds, migraine, and wind, and bad chronic head sores, and how to cut them as I describe.
And this is by shaving the head of the sick person and scraping his back in the place of the two veins with a rough rag, then strangle the neck of the sick person with a headscarf until the two veins appear and their place behind the ears in the low places of the head, so you will feel them with your finger and where you felt their pulse under your finger, there mark it with the pen.
Then take a sharp scalpel, which is known as the "nishl," and insert it under the armpit into the skin until the scalpel reaches the bone. Then raise your hand with the armpit and skin upwards and cut the artery with the skin in a clean cut, and the length of the cut should be like two closed fingers.
Or so, and send from the blood as much as you desire, then tie them with sutures and leave them until they heal.  Previously in the beginning of the book, I have mentioned cutting and cauterizing them. As for the two arteries that are in the temples, their usefulness is to cut them for chronic headache, difficult headache, constant ophthalmia and acute discharge that flows sharply toward the eyes.
And how to cut them as I describe to you, the patient ties his neck with a turban until the arteries appear to the touch clearly and their pulse is felt under your finger. Then, learn by the pencil mark, and raise the skin from above the artery upward with your index finger.
And insert the "nishl" scalpel from below and raise the artery upwards, cutting it as you did with the other arteries, and send from the blood as much as needed, then release the patient's neck constriction and place your finger on the artery for an hour.
Then place cotton and sutures on it, and tighten them firmly above, leaving him until he heals.  Previously I have mentioned their cutting and cauterization in the beginning of the book. As for severing the artery of the forehead, its benefit is after severing the cephalic artery for chronic ailments of the face such as erysipelas, ulcers, and severe redness.
And how to cut it as I describe to you, the patient ties his neck with a turban until the artery appears. Then take the instrument called the "fasa," and this is its image:
Place the spike that is at the head of the "fasa" on the same artery and strike from above with a comb or something similar to it, and leave the blood to flow as much as you desire. Then release the patient's neck constriction and tighten it until he heals.
It may be opened with a wide scalpel, but it should not be sharp-pointed like other scalpels; rather, its tip should be slightly blunt and its edge sharpened on the curved side because the bone is near, so perhaps the scalpel would break if it were thin.
But as for the two veins in the hollows of the eyes, their use is for the ailments of the eye from scabies, redness, and itchiness, and diseases of the face. As for how to cut them, it is that the sick person holds his neck tight with a turban, then they are cut while you stand on his head and let the incision be slightly curved to length using a small scalpel that is slightly wide.
For the place has no flesh in it, so if the scalpel is thin-tipped, it may break. Then a cloth is placed on them and bound for one night after sending off the blood, then they are removed. As for phlebotomy from the nose vein, it is beneficial for acute fever.

So you open them and be careful not to overdo cutting them because there are arteries underneath them, and perhaps a bleeding from those arteries will occur, and as for the three veins that are opened in the elbow, they are the ones that have become customary to open them in all people, and opening them is done in two ways.
Either a cut with a wide or olive-shaped scalpel into the throat, or a cut with a thin scalpel, which is called trepanning (and this is its image), an image of the wide, rehani scalpel.
It will be wide as you see, so that it is suitable for opening the swollen, prominent, visible veins which contain thick blood like tar, and this is an image of the olive-shaped scalpel.
And this scalpel is narrower and has a thinner tip, suitable for letting blood from fine veins containing thin, yellowish blood, and this is an image of the trepanning scalpel.
This trepan, which is suitable for cutting and has types of wide and narrow ones according to the width and narrowness of the veins, and this can be deduced from it, and among artisans it is famous.
As for the basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, its benefit when it is let is that it attracts blood from illnesses that are under the throat and neck to what follows the chest and abdomen, and it is necessary for the phlebotomist when he lets blood to be careful and be on the neck of it because beneath it there is an artery, so if he makes a mistake and increases in cutting with the scalpel, he will cut that artery.
For this reason, it is necessary that his letting of blood not be with a cutting scalpel but rather be with a trepan, and if the basilic vein does not appear clearly, then it is necessary to avoid it and turn to another or request some of its branches.

If you see the blood when you bleed, gushing up like the urine of a boy and the blood is thin and red, know that it is from the artery. Then quickly put your finger on it for a long time, then remove your finger. If the blood stops, most often it will stop, so tighten the arm.

But that swelling does not harm it at all. As for the method of phlebotomy and its effects, and what should be taken into consideration in its repair, first of all it is necessary to know that phlebotomy is either used only for maintaining health.
Or it is used for diseases, and the one that is used to maintain health and prevent disease should be phlebotomized in one of the three veins in the arm, namely the cephalic, basilic, and brachial, and it should be phlebotomized at the beginning of spring when signs of fullness appear.
And phlebotomy should be done on Sunday or Tuesday after three hours have passed since sunrise. As for the phlebotomy that is used for diseases, it does not have a limited time, but whenever need and urgency call for it from night or day.
And in every hour and every time, and it is not necessary to phlebotomize boys until they reach fourteen years of age, nor old men who have passed sixty years of age. If anyone intends to phlebotomize for any reason...

One should also be cautious with venesection after diarrhea, vomiting, and childbirth, as well as excessive intercourse, fatigue, exercise, wakefulness, fasting, and anything that weakens the body, whether physically or mentally. Then examine the thinning of the humors before that, if the blood is thick.
And he should eat and drink and use medicine if possible, then enter the bath if it does not prevent him, or engage in some exercise so that the blood flows and makes its venesection in the middle of the day as we mentioned, and he aims to empty his chest that day of all bad psychological conditions.
Like worries, anger, fear, and all physical conditions such as excessive fatigue, strain, intercourse, and the like, and prepare his meeting with things that have become customary among people to use from types of perfume, incense, entertainment, and the like, each person according to what he is able.
Then he makes the venesection on a pillow that is higher than the pillow on which the one being bled sits, then he extends his arm and rubs it with his hand two or three times, then he tightens the band with the tourniquet and winds it two or three times, and let the tightening be moderate.
Because tightening, when it is not moderate, either by excessive tightening prevents the flow of blood, or if it is too loose it also prevents the flow of blood, then after tightening it is necessary that the one being bled rubs his hands together both with each other and lightly.
Until the veins swell and become clear to the touch, then he wipes the scalpel with a little of old oil, especially, then places his index finger of his left hand on the same vein under the place where he wants to make the venesection slightly so that the vein does not recede and avoid the strike. Because some of the veins are found like cords that recede during venesection and some of them are full of air, so when the scalpel is placed on it, it goes down under the scalpel and deceives the venesector and does not open the vein, or if he opens it, then its opening will be narrow.
Therefore, it is necessary that the incisions be firm and careful in all these matters, then the scalpel descends if the opening of the vein from its place (is) passed, otherwise he aids it another time below that place a little or above it quickly if the place does not swell.

And I fear that the blood will not exit in the quantity you want, so return the scalpel to the same hole gently on a straight line and increase the opening slightly and do this quickly before the place swells because many people may swell at the small incision.

If a large swelling occurs at the site of the incision, which happens often especially to those who have not been bled before or whose opening of the vein was small, act quickly and place on the swelling a sponge soaked in warm water and salt and tighten it for an hour because it will dissolve.
It is necessary to do this after the blood has exited from the vein itself in its entirety or from another vein, if something of black or green remains in the place after days, it does not harm, if you wish, load on it something of patience and bitter solvent.
Put nothing from the juice of al-fudnagh and the like on it, for a swelling and tumor often occurs when bloodletting is performed in the bassilyca. Place your hand upon it; if you find it throbbing when you press on it, that is an evil tumor, so beware that you place nothing of what has been mentioned on it.
For it may be that blood flows from a vein, but clot it with what is in it so that it hardens the place, and then treat it with all other treatment until it heals. It is proper to draw out of each person's blood according to his strength and the color that appears to prevail on the blood.
For if the blood is black, let it be drawn out until it becomes red, and likewise if you see it thick, send it until it becomes thin, and likewise if it is sharp until its sharpness goes away. It is proper for one who is full of strength and needs to have his blood drawn all at once.
That he widen the bloodletting of the armpit and that the lancet be wide, and for one who is weak, the opposite of this. It is proper to draw it out in several times and that the hole be narrow. The best thing to use in bloodletting of the armpit is that it be twisted, boiled, a slit and not a piercing.
And this kind of bloodletting is safe from bleeding and cutting a nerve, and it is better and safer than bloodletting by breadth and length, and for one who is accustomed at bloodletting to the covering over the area, it is proper to feed him before bloodletting something of bread soaked in pomegranate water or vinegar.
If he is feverish and blood has been drawn from him three or four times, if he is cold-natured, let him take before bloodletting bread soaked in the drink of honey, or a drink of honey flavored with hawthorn berries, or the fragrant thyme drink.

That which is due to bleeding, and as for one who desires to comfort his arm and relax his blood a second time, it befits him who had a cut to bleed little by little according to strength over consecutive days.

And as for one who had in his body blood much, that heated and intensified and caused fever, it befits that blood is drawn from him in one rush and a large quantity is removed and the incision is widened until the white coat appears after ensuring all conditions of venesection have been met. Chapter Sixty-Nine.
On cupping and how to use it.
Cupping instruments can be made of horn, wood, copper, and glass. Cupping is done in two ways: one with a condition involving the drawing of blood, and the other without a condition.
And this cupping that is without a condition is done in two ways.
And cupping the sides of the neck, which are both sides of the neck altogether, and cupping the chin, which is below the lower jaw from the mouth, and cupping the shoulders, and cupping the lower back on the coccyx, and cupping the forearms, which are in the middle of the arms, and cupping the legs, and cupping the ankles.
Cupping draws blood from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and for that reason, it does not draw strength as venesection does, and cupping should not be used with fire or without fire in any of the diseases that are from fullness until the body expels everything.
If need requires cupping from illness or by habit, we use it at any time in the beginning of the month and at its end and in the middle of it and in any time it was.
That is because some people, if their blood increases greatly to the point where they need to remove it by cupping, find heaviness and headache in their head. And some of them feel fullness and redness in their face and head and neck, and some feel itching on their face and cheek and darkness and swelling in their eyes, and some feel an itch in the place where they were cupped.
Some laugh a great deal, and some find the taste of blood in their mouths, and their gums bleed, and they spit blood. And some sleep a lot, and some see in their dreams blood, redness, wounds, and surgeries, and things like that. So when we see any of these things
And especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we order cupping after two or three hours have passed from noon. As for the benefit of cupping the nape, it benefits from heaviness in the head and water that flows into the eyes.
But it should be after the body has emptied its contents, and this cupping may be a substitute for cephalic venesection. It is cautioned against using it by those who are cold-headed or have a cold, as it will harm them greatly.
Therefore, it should not be used by the elderly or those with cold-headed illnesses, and whoever habitually uses it develops forgetfulness.
Therefore, it is necessary to order the cupper to lower his hand with the cup slightly downwards, for fear of forgetfulness being produced. As for cupping the shoulder, it is a substitute for phlebotomy at the armpit and bloodletting at the brachial vein.
Therefore, it benefits from asthma, shortness of breath, constriction of the respiratory apparatus, cough, and fullness. It is necessary to raise cupping of the shoulder slightly because if it is lowered downwards, it produces weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for cupping the nape of the neck, it benefits from pains occurring in the head, ophthalmia, migraine, angina pectoris, and pain at the roots of the teeth. It is a substitute for bloodletting at the brachial vein, and it is necessary to order the cupper not to deepen his hand with the cup so that he does not cut a vessel and cause bleeding.
As for cupping under the chin, it benefits from sores in the mouth, gum disease, and similar diseases in the mouth. It may replace phlebotomy at the jowls. As for cupping the shoulders, it benefits from palpitations that occur due to fullness and heat.
As for cupping the abdomen of the groin, it is beneficial in what it benefits by obstructing the three veins: the basilik, akhal, and qifal, because it draws blood from all those fine veins in the flesh and draws those fine veins from other thicker veins until the drawing reaches the three thick veins.
The cupper should be ordered not to be excessive in the application, because the place is exposed of flesh and under it are nerves and arteries.  As for the single cupping that is applied on the coccyx, it benefits from hemorrhoids of the seat and sores of the lower part.
It is necessary to order the cupper that the cupping be large and made of brass because the place needs a strong suction and perhaps a glass cupping might break and it requires many conditions, as for the cupping of the legs it diminishes fullness noticeably.
Because it draws blood from the entire body and benefits from chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder, it induces menstruation and benefits from boils and carbuncles and performs the function of obstructing the saphenous and femoral veins, except that it depletes the body greatly and causes giddiness in most people. Cupping of the femoral veins benefits similarly to cupping of the legs.
The method of placing the cupping is that the cupping is placed first empty and it sucks a moderate suction, but you do not prolong the placement of the cupping but place it quickly and remove it quickly to accept the fluids into the place steadily and continue to repeat that until you see the place has reddened and opened.
And when redness of blood appeared, then it is stipulated and suction is resumed gradually, and then the state of the bodies is looked at. From those people whose flesh was soft and porous with holes, it is necessary to stipulate one cupping only so that the place does not become chafed.
The surgeon must widen the incision and deepen it slightly and adjust the tissue gently with care and movement. If there is thick blood, it is necessary to make an incision twice. In the first incision, he should open a way for the flow of blood and its fluids. And in the second incision, it is for thoroughly removing the thick blood.
If the blood is very thick, he repeats the incision a third time to achieve the goal. In general, if we want to remove a small amount of blood, we are content with one incision. If we want to remove a large amount of blood, we make more incisions. And if we consider the blood to be thick.
It is necessary to make a deep incision and the moderate depth of the incision is only the depth of the skin. It is not necessary to use ointments when placing ligatures and what the surgeon warns against. As for those whose skin is thick, firm, coarse, and with narrow pores.
It is necessary to lubricate the places of ligatures with softening, melting, and dissolving ointments.  But if it is summer time, such as the ointment of khari, violet ointment, almond sweet oil, or pumpkin seed oil.
And if it is winter, such as daffodil ointment, lily ointment, chamomile ointment, or hyacinth and the like. If the fat is thick and cold, let the ointment be marjoram ointment, fig ointment, banana oil, or sandalwood oil and so on.
If the surgeon has wide pores and thick flesh, it is necessary to abstain from ointment. These people should wash their ligatures after cupping with rose water or cold water or grape vine water or pumpkin water or  watering of men and so on. And as for those whose blood is very moist.
They wash their ligatures with vinegar or  water of ash and mastic and so on. And as for those whose fat is thick, they wash their ligatures with old syrup or marjoram water or sandalwood broth or chamomile and so on. It is necessary to avoid cupping in the bath and after the bath.
It is not necessary to use it after coming out of the bath for an hour or two, and it is not permissible for anyone to sleep after cupping, what should be taken care of by the physician and the patient before cupping and after it.
It is necessary to first look if the cupper or the patient is jaundiced and the majority of his blood is on the edge and inflammation, then it is necessary for him to take cooling things like pomegranates and dandelion with vinegar, lettuce, chicory, and molasses and so on, and he should make his food quail, lamb skewers, and hare meat and so on.
And if his temperament is cold, it is necessary for him to drink honey syrup, miwa syrup, or molasses, and he should take the aromatic wine that is between old and new, and he should be ordered to eat little food, and he should make his food quail, partridge, sparrows, and pigeon chicks, skewers, and it is necessary for the drink on the day of cupping and venesection to be more than food.
And it may be necessary to give some people, at times,  of the Farouk elixir or musk medicine or shieltha before cupping and venesection or after it to strengthen the main organs and thin the blood, and it is not permissible to give it to those who are feverish.
As for the cupping that is without condition, it is the cupping that is placed on the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, kidney location, and hip because these organs do not tolerate conditions upon them, but rather it is intended to draw blood from one organ to another.
Such as placing cupping on the breasts in case of hemorrhoids, or we use it to dissolve the cold air that has lodged in the organ, such as placing cupping on the abdomen and navel, because it loosens the organ, warms it, and removes the pain to dissolve it.
And it may be placed on the kidneys if there is a blockage or stone in them, with strong pulling it may open the blockage or remove the stone from its place, and it also does this if placed on the liver and spleen when wind is disturbed in them, and these instruments may be used empty with only pressing.
And it may be used with fire and it may be used filled with lukewarm water in the case of  sores, as it is filled and made large with hot water alone or with water that has been cooked with some herbs suitable for this purpose.
Then it is placed filled on the place and held and removed and repeated as needed, and this is an image of the instrument used with fire:
Its mouth opening is two fingers wide as we have pictured it and its depth is half a cubit, and there is a small hole on the side of it, about halfway through, the size that a needle can enter, made from Chinese copper or yellow brass with a thick rim.
Flat and smooth, so as not to harm the limb when placed upon it, and there is a crossbar in its center made of copper or iron where the candle with fire is placed, and this instrument may be made large larger than what we have described and small according to the diseases.
And the instrument used is that instruments for boys and the slender are not instruments for men and strong bodies, and as for how this instrument is placed with fire on the limb, it is brought to a flame with tightly woven cotton or a small candle from wax, and it is placed on the middle of the erected penis which is in the middle of the instrument.
So that the fire rises upward toward the bottom of the instrument so that it does not burn the body of the sick person, then it is placed on the limb and the finger on the hole we mentioned until when we hold the instrument as much as needed we remove the finger.
And steam comes out through that hole and the instrument dissolves on the place, then the candle is brought to a flame in the same way and it is repeated if needed, as for the instrument used in sores with water, there is no erected penis or hole.
It is used by filling it with water and placing it on the member only, and this is its shape: .
And this vessel, whenever it is large enough to hold a lot of water, is better,.
Chapter Seventy-Seven.
On the Removal of A foreign Body.
A foreign body is used in most cases for the organs where it's impossible to use vessels either because they are small like the lip and gums, or because the organ is exposed of flesh like a finger and nose, etc..
And the way to use it is by choosing a foreign body that is in clean freshwater, free from impurities, then leaving it for a day and night in clean water until it starves and nothing remains inside its cavity. Then the body is evacuated first by venesection or cupping, then the diseased organ is wiped.
Until it reddens, then it is placed on it and if it fills up, it falls off. If it is possible to suck the place with a vessel, it is more effective in benefit; otherwise, wash the place with vinegar then with much water and massage and squeeze it. If bleeding continues after the removal of the foreign body and was a discharge, then soak a cotton cloth in cold water.
And place it on top until the discharge stops. If the bleeding is excessive, sprinkle powdered saffron or a similar astringent powder on it until the bleeding stops, or half of the peeled bulbils are placed on the affected area.
And leave it until the bulbils stick to the area, then the bleeding stops. It is necessary that if reapplication of the foreign body is needed, do not use the same foreign body if another is possible; if the foreign body refuses to adhere, wipe the area with fresh blood.
Or insert a needle into the area until something of blood comes out, then place it; if it feels anything of blood, it will stick to the place. If you want to remove it, sprinkle some frankincense or salt or ash on it and it will fall off from its place.
The Third Chapter.
On Fractures.
This chapter also belongs to the knowledge necessary in the craft of medicine, namely the setting of fractures and dislocations that occur in the bones.
Know, O sons of men, that this chapter may be claimed by the ignorant among physicians and laymen and those who have never browsed even a single book on it from antiquity or read even a letter from it.
And because of this reason, this art has become commonplace in our land as a matter of science, and indeed I have never encountered anyone skilled in it at all. And what I have derived from it is due to my long reading of the books of antiquity and my diligence in understanding them until I extracted the knowledge of it from them, and then I adhered to practice and experience throughout my life.
And I have drawn for you in this chapter all that encompasses my knowledge of it. And my experience has passed after I have brought it closer to you and condensed it from the prolixity of  length and shortened it to the utmost brevity, and explained it to the greatest extent of clarity, and depicted for you many images of the instruments used in it.
Since it is an increase in clarification, as I have done in the preceding chapters, and there is no power except with Allah, the Most High, the Great.
The First Section.
In it are the beauty and summaries of the matter of fractures of bones, and it is necessary to present them before we begin to mention the broken and dislocated members one by one, it is fitting that in the beginning of this chapter, we mention a summary of the saying and sections. It is necessary for you first to understand them and stand on their reality, you and anyone who is eager to learn this noble craft, other than yourselves.
So I say that when a fracture, dislocation, or sprain or fall occurs to someone, it is necessary first to hasten to its separation or loosening or both of them. If nothing prevents it, such as weakness of strength or if the one afflicted by it is a child or an old man, whether it be during extreme heat or extreme cold.
Then he should restrict his food to cold legumes, poultry and hare meat, and abstain from drink, heavy meats, overeating, and all food that fills the veins with blood until the swelling subsides and there is no expectation of fluid seeping into the area.
Then, when this happens, let the sick person return to his usual way of life. But if the broken bone is in a process of setting, it is necessary for the sick person to eat foods that are rich and thick and have a lot of fat, like rice, barley, heads, roots, and veal marrow. And eggs, fatty fish and thick drinks, as these foods hasten and improve the setting of the fracture.  Know that if a bone is broken in robust men or old men, it will never completely heal to its original state.
Because their bones are dry and stiff, and although it may heal, it will not be like the very soft bones of young children. However nature grows a substance on all sides of the broken bone that resembles glue. It thickens and binds it until the pieces adhere to one another.
And it binds the pieces together until it becomes as strong and firm as it was originally, so that nothing hinders his actions.
For this reason, it is necessary to give the sick person food that contains strength, fat and thickness as we have mentioned. Know that fractures can vary in types depending on the different bones because a broken leg bone is different from a broken skull bone, and a broken rib bone is different from a broken spine bone.
And so with all the other bones, each is different from the others. And we will discuss each type of fracture in its own chapter, in detail and separately from the others.  And a type of bone fracture can also vary within itself.
For it may be a fracture that is a clean break without fragments, and it may be a fracture along the length of the bone and have fragments and protrusions, both attached and detached. And the fracture may be accompanied by a wound and tearing in the skin, and the fracture may be a slight fissure.
And for each type there is a special method of healing it, as will be mentioned in detail in its place. And what distinguishes a broken bone is its bending and deviation and its appearance to the touch and its creaking when you move it with your hand.
But when there is no visible bending or creaking or feeling of disturbance when you touch the bone, and the patient does not feel much pain, then there is no fracture, but it can be a sprain or a slight fracture or a slight fissure, so it should not be moved by stretching or probing at all.
But apply to it the medicines that will be mentioned later, which are appropriate for the location, and then gently bandage it. And know that if a bone is broken and shattered into two parts without fragments, except that each part has moved from its place.
Then you must hasten from that time to straighten and set it before a hot swelling occurs, for if a hot swelling occurs, leave it for days until the hot swelling subsides, then straighten it in any way you can with gentleness and ingenuity.
And know that its healing and straightening is easier than the bone that has fragments. Then it is bound as will be mentioned later, but if the bone has fragments, then there is a necessity to stretch the broken limb from both sides, whether it be an arm or a leg.
Either with your hands if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or with a rope and hand, and let the position of the limb be on a level surface according to its natural shape until when the broken part of the bone has stretched, then at that time you return those protrusions to their places in every way you can with skill and gentleness.
Strive with all your effort that you do not inflict pain or suffering on the sick by your action, nor grief. Strive that you set one bone next to its fellow in the best form. And it is necessary at that time to touch them and feel them with your hand. If you see something amiss there, correct and smooth it as much as your strength allows.
Beware of excessive force and strong pulling as many ignorant people do, and often boast about doing so, causing a hot or cold sensation in the member, as I have repeatedly seen them do.
Then after setting and perfecting, and tightening that member, impose stillness and repose on it, and warn the sick person not to move it during his waking or sleeping time, or when he is shifting or agitated, or when he strains himself in all his movements as far as possible, and that he should take care for the position of the member to be a position with which he feels safe from pain.
For when he feels pain or discomfort in that position, he should move it to a different position until he feels no pain, and take care that that position be level and straight.
Lest there be a deformity in the member if it is forced, and as for how to tighten the broken member, it is what I describe to you. Know that broken members differ in their smallness and largeness and forms; those which are small like the arm, finger, and hand, etc.
Those which are small like the arm, finger, and hand, etc., should be bound with bandages of thin soft cloth, but those that are large like the thigh, back, and chest, should be bound with firmer, sturdier bandages because a wide bandage is necessary for the larger member and binds it from all sides with an equal tension without any defect.
Then after you have finished setting, begin to carry the appropriate plaster. Over the site of the fracture, then begin to wrap the bandage around the site of the fracture itself, three or four times, depending on what the member deserves. Pull your hand slightly with the bandage, then move it upward from the site of the fracture.
Pull with less force than you would for the fractured location, then twist it away from the fracture site slightly and gradually relax the tension until you take a piece of good tissue from the correct position, then take another bandage and wrap it also around the broken area.
Wrap it, then take the wrapping to the lower side of the fracture and let your action in tightening and loosening the wrap be as we mentioned in the first upper wrap, then place between the wraps a soft fabric or linen that is equal to leveling the curvature of the fracture.
If there is a curvature, otherwise do not put anything in it, then wrap another bandage on it and then smooth on these wraps the firm splint from your watch if there is no swelling or hot tumor in the limb.
If there is swelling or a hot tumor, put on it what soothes that tumor and removes the swelling and leave it for some days, then tighten the splint on it then and let the splints be from halves of carved reeds prepared skillfully or let the splints be from acacia wood.
Which are made from cedar or palm leaves or flax or hawthorn and the like, of what is available of that, and let the width of each splint be on this form and this shape exactly.
Unless the splint that is placed on the fracture itself be a little thicker and wider than the other splints, but as for the length of the splint, it is made according to what suits the fractured limb from its size, image of the splint:.
Then you tighten the splints with another bandage according to your first tightening, and then tie it from above with firm threads according to what we mentioned about the tension, which is that your tightening on the fracture site be more. And every time you move away from the fracture, the tension is less.
And the threads should be medium in thickness and softness, and they should be made of damp cotton, especially because if the threads are thick, as you see from the actions of ignorants who make their threads from frayed cotton strips, which is a great mistake.
Because the tension with it is excessive and the fine threads are not suitable because you do not reach what you want by tightening with them, and it should not be less than a finger between the bandage and the bandage.
If the patient is harmed by the edges of the bandages after tightening in the correct places, make under them from soft padding or fluffy wool so that it does not harm him from that anything, and as for if the fracture is with a wound and there is a rupture in the skin, we will come to it separately.
And know that not every broken member should be tightened with bandages from the first day, because if the limb is large, it should not be bandaged until after five days or seven or more according to your safety from the occurrence of hot swelling.
And this is the description of the bandages that were used to bandage the early fractures, dislocations, and sprains, a general bandage that shortens the fracture and is suitable for most temperaments, especially children and women because it does not overcome heat or cold.
And that is to take from the flour dust, which is the fine powder that clings to the walls of the mill when the millstone moves, and you knead it as it is without mixing it with egg white, and make its dough neither thick nor thin, then you cover it.
Description of another bandage that bandages the fracture, dislocation, and sprain, take from the mastic, lead, acacia, resin, marshmallow, and saffron from each ten drachmas, and frankincense and myrrh from each five drachmas, and from the wool twenty drachmas, and from the Armenian or Roman clay twenty drachmas. Pound everything and mix with wool water or egg white if the patient's temperament is hot, then this bandage is used because it is good in composition, binds broken bones quickly, and is suitable for most people because of its moderation.
A plaster which is also beneficial for fractures and dislocations, is taken of wool ten drachmas, linen ten drachmas, white lead ten drachmas, resin five drachmas, frankincense six drachmas, yellow earth twenty drachmas. All are finely ground and sifted and kneaded with water or egg white and used.
A plaster for joints and bones that have gone astray from their places, and quiets the pain accompanying them, and also for fractured bone and broken bones, is taken.  Woven wool is immersed in vinegar and cooked oil and placed on the site. This plaster has no binding force.
But it is excellent in quieting the hot swelling and relieving pain, especially. A plaster for binding a fractured bone is taken.  Dried fig leaves and dried poppy leaves are finely ground and applied with them moist. A brief plaster used when binding a broken bone.
And if you want to analyze the remaining swelling, take from each part of lead white, chamomile, violets, and powdered saffron, grind all together fine and knead with paint. If the limb is not stiff then knead with water or coriander water and use it.
A stronger plaster for dissolving this one is used when a solid swelling occurs when binding a bone. Take from each part of lead white, cottonseed, fenugreek, crown imperial, wormwood, violets, and chamomile.
Grind everything together and knead with  diluted vinegar or fresh water or paint, all according to the heat of the limb and its stillness. But Hippocrates did not mention in his book that it is placed on the broken limb when binding it.
Except the cerate made of wax and oil only, and he described it to be medium between thick and thin. But Galen saw that on the broken limb when binding it, things containing resins with something from heat like resin, frankincense, and similar to what we mentioned.
A plaster that is beneficial for weakness and pain, take barley, lentils, human hair or bird feathers,  a small amount of flaxseed meal and salt in equal parts, grind finely, sift and knead together and apply it as a plaster. As for the measure that should remain tied.
Then it should be loosened, and if the patient does not experience pain, itching or movement of the broken bone from its place, do not loosen it for many days. If severe itching, disturbing pain or swelling occurs in the location then
then act quickly and loosen it at once and do not delay that at all, and remove the plaster from him, then take a soft cloth or sponge of seawater, moistened in warm water, and wash the place with it until the itching subsides and the pain ceases, then leave the limb to rest for an hour.
Then cover it with soft wool soaked in vinegar and oil or rose butter, and bind it on him for a night until the hot swelling is prevented, and the swelling of the limb subsides and its pain goes away, then return it to mild tightness and light plaster and do not tighten it as you did at first.
And treat it until it heals, if you see that the swelling, redness, pain and puffiness and all symptoms have disappeared entirely and he needs plaster and tightness, then redo it as you did at first, whether it is in equal amounts, if nothing of what has been mentioned happens in the limb.
then do not loosen it except after three or four or five or seven days, and he may leave it for twenty days, all of that according to what appears to you from the condition of the limb as we have said until when the fracture is necessary and the flesh near healing.
Then also, at that time, increase the tightness more than your first tightening, and also increase the thickness of the patient's food as mentioned before, if you see that the fracture site has dried up and loosened more than necessary, know that food is prevented from reaching it.
So let him bathe it in lukewarm water at every time he attends to it and let that be every three days, and slacken the bandage a little for with this act nourishment flows to the member and it heals quickly, as for what ignoramuses do of those who force (the broken) member again.
For if it is not set properly at first and then forced into a crooked shape, that is an error from them and a great evil. And even if it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and practiced it, but I have not seen any trace of that from any of them at all. And the right thing is to not do it.
Chapter Two.
In fractures that occur in the head.
The types of fractures that occur in the head are many and their shapes are diverse and their causes are varied, for some fracture is from a sword blow and it may be that the bone breaks completely until it reaches the peritoneum which is under the bone. Just as the carpenter does in wood.
And for this reason this type of fracture is called 'carpenter-like', and it may be that the sword cuts some of the bone and breaks its surface only and does not penetrate to the end of it, and this type of fracture is called a 'loose fracture', and the wound of these two fractures is either large or small.
And from fractures some are crushing or bruises and their cause is a blow with a stone or a fall on a stone or something similar, and this fracture may be penetrating having nearly reached the membrane under the bone, or it may be in the surface of the bone.
And the wound of these two fractures is also either large or small, and from fractures some are hidden in the bone to a thinness like hair which is a slight crack.
And for this reason this type of fracture is called 'hair-like', and from it, there is a fracture that occurs from a fall or the throw of a stone and similar things that enter the page of the bone inside and make the place concave like what happens to a piece of copper if it is hit by a blow and part of it enters inside.
Most of these fractures occur in the soft skull bones, such as those of infants. All these types of fracture may have clean and un-clean fragments. And I will mention their treatment in its place, and we recognize all these types of fractures by examining them.
And examining them with probes and extracting any rotten flesh from them. As for the hairline fracture, it is known that by exposing the bone and wiping it and then rubbing black lead on it, the fracture appears black.  As for the treatment of a fracture, one must first look at the symptoms of the patient.
If you see from his symptoms what clearly indicates fear, such as vomiting bile and expansion and loss of mind and cessation of voice and fainting and severe fever and protrusion and redness of the eyes and similar symptoms, do not approach the sick person nor treat him, for death is inevitable to him in most cases with these symptoms.
But if you see symptoms that do not frighten you and you hope for his recovery, then take him in hand to treat him, because if the injured person comes to you at the very beginning of his injury and that was during winter.
then it is necessary to exert yourself in removing the bone before the fourteenth day, at all events.
