And if it was during summer, it is necessary to hasten in removing the bone before the seventh day, before what is under the bone of the membrane spoils and exposes those symptoms that we described.
If the fracture of the bone reached the membrane covering the brain and was accompanied by crushing and bruising, then it is necessary to cut off the crushed part as I describe it to you, which is to shave the head of the wounded patient.
And expose the bone in any way you are able, according to the shape of the wound and what is hidden from the patient, if when examining the bone you find bleeding or a warm swelling, then respond to that as necessary.
This is done by stuffing the site with a piece of linen soaked in syrup and rose oil, and cleaning the wound until the swelling subsides and bleeding stops, then taking to setting the bone and removing it which is done in two ways.
As for one way, it is to cut the bone with a sharp blade of fine width and its image is: .
Then you use another cutting tool after this one, wider than it slightly, and its image is:
Then you also use another cutting tool, wider than the second one, and it is necessary for you to have several different cutters, some wider than others and some shorter than others, and their edges should be extremely sharp, and they should be made of Indian steel or good steel.
And use the handle to strike on the cutter so that it does not shake the head and harm it, and if the bone is strong and solid, then it is necessary to drill around it before using the cutters with holes called non-closed holes, rather than closed ones.
Because they do not exceed the limit of the skull bone to what is beyond it, because the drill has a circular shape below its sharp head similar to a collar or small circle that prevents it from diving and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
And it is necessary for you to take several of these holes, each suitable for the thickness of that bone, until it prepares for every skull a sharpened tip in length and shortness according to the thickness of that skull, and this is an image of three types. Of the drills large, medium, and small:
And as for the way to drill around the broken bone, it is to place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you feel that the bone has been penetrated, then move the drill to another location and make a distance between each hole equal to the thickness of a thread or similar.
Then cut between each pair of holes with the utmost care as we have said until you remove the bone either with your hand or with something else from among the instruments you have prepared for this purpose.
Like the awl and the blunt probes, and it is necessary that you exercise all caution that the hole or cut does not touch anything of the peritoneum. And if the bone is strengthened and separated from the peritoneum if it was stuck to it, and you have removed it, then it is necessary that you strip it.
And make smooth the roughness that remains in the bone with another instrument similar to the cutter except that it should be more delicate and gentler than all the other cutters, and if anything of the small bones and fragments remain take them gently with what you have of instruments.
Then treat the wound after that with the poultices and ointments mentioned, and as for the other face of the work it is a face easy to understand, far from ambiguity, which Galen mentioned and praised highly, this is his saying. It is necessary for you first to begin by exposing the part of the bone from the location where the fracture is most severe and well-known until, when you have exposed that part, you place the tip of this cutting tool under it and this is its image:
The blunt part of it is smooth, not cutting anything and the sharp part of it on its sides going in length as you see so that the blunt part rests against the peritoneum and the face of the sharp part on the bone, then strike the cutter from one side.
With a small mallet until you cut all the bone gently as it rotates and while you are safe from the membrane, no frightening incident occurs at all even if the surgeon is the most ignorant and coward of people, yes even if he was asleep.
If anything of the membrane is left attached to the bone in some locations of the bone, scrape it off with the blunt end of the cutter itself and remove it gently for it comes off without harm or fear.
As for a fracture of the bone that has not penetrated the membrane and the portion that was severed from the bone is its surface, and there remains roughness and fragments, it is necessary to remove that roughness and pull out those fragments with tweezers made of different sizes so you can use them in every place appropriately and best according to what guides you.
And for the same broken bone and its shape, it is necessary that in removing its roughness you first use the broadest tweezers, then after that one finer than it, and continue doing so until you reach using the finest and most delicate of them all.
As for the rest of the small cracks, fine fissures, and slight fractures, it is necessary to use a treatment for each one according to what leads to its healing. This is something that is not hidden from anyone who has even the slightest skill in this craft and who has paid attention to what we have written and explained about major fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed when you remove the bone, it is necessary to take a cotton swab equal in size to the wound and immerse it in rose oil and place it on the mouth of the wound, then take another folded or tripled cotton swab. And immerse it in syrup and rose oil and place it on the first swab, and do this as lightly as you can so as not to burden the peritoneum, then apply a wide bandage over it and do not tighten it except to the extent that it only holds the wound.
And let it remain with this treatment for one or two days until you are certain of the hot swelling, then remove the bandage and use after that the medicines that come with it such as dried lilies, ground caraway seeds, finely ground coriander, and saffron, and the like - which is to make a plaster from these medicines.
And apply to the wound a dry plaster made with them, and in general, use a medicine that is cleansing and not irritating.  Be careful when treating the wound that it is clean, free of grease, dirt, and do not allow pus to accumulate in it at all.
For if pus collects on the membrane of the brain, it will damage and corrupt it; this will cause great harm to the sick person, and it may occur in the cavity of the skull when the bone is exposed, especially if treatment is neglected.
If you look at the surface and see blackness, if it presents the symptoms we mentioned, know that he is inevitably doomed. If the blackness happened because of a medicine applied to it, and that medicine was potent enough to cause that blackness.
It is necessary to take from the honey a part and from rose oil three parts, beat them well together, and smear with it a cloth and then place it on the cavity of the skull and treat him with all kinds of treatment until he heals.
Chapter Three.
In setting a broken nose.
Know that the nose does not break except the upper part of it, all or one of them, because they are bone; as for the lower part of it, it is cartilaginous and does not break but it may be injured, bent and broken.
If one of the parts breaks, it is necessary to insert your little finger into the nostril and straighten that fracture from inside with your index finger and thumb from outside until you return the nose to its natural shape. Let this be done gently by you and take care that what you do does not cause pain to the sick person.
If the fracture is at the top of the nose and the finger cannot reach it, it must be straightened with a blunt instrument wrapped in a cloth; if the fracture is on both sides, do the same thing. He should hasten to set it on the first day of the fracture.
If it is possible, otherwise after the seventh or tenth day when the hot swelling subsides, then insert a wick of cotton thread into the nostril if the fracture is on one side, or two wicks if the fracture is on both sides.
Let the size of the plug be as thick as that which fills the nasal hole, and some of the early proctors said that the plug should be moistened with butter and changed every day, but I do not see this, rather it is appropriate to moisten the plug in egg white mixed with ground barley dust.
Then the plug is left until the bone sets and the cartilage hardens, and a tube of goose feathers may be inserted into the nasal passage after it is wrapped in a fine linen cloth, so that the retention of the broken nose becomes stronger and lest the patient be unable to breathe.
And this is not something necessary, if you wish to make it and if you wish to make the plugs, if a warm swelling appears in the nose during your work, apply cold to the nose with quince or cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil or something from the Diacylon ointment.
If no warm swelling appears, it is necessary to apply a compress from the outside with semolina and crushed coriander seeds, mixed with egg white, then put a soft bandage on it and do not tie the nose to anything at all, if the nasal bones are broken in a small fracture.
Or if it is fragmented, it is necessary to cut into it and remove it with the instrument suitable for it, then sew up the incision and treat it with ointments that promote healing and closure, if a wound occurs inside the nose, it must be treated with a plug and use lead pipes. Until it heals.
Chapter Four.
In setting a broken jaw.
If the lower jaw is broken and it is not broken with a wound, if its fracture is from the outside only and it does not break in two and protrudes inward, then knowing it is easy.
Then, if the fracture is in the right section, it is necessary to insert the index finger of the left hand into the mouth of the patient and likewise if the fracture is in the left section, insert the index finger of the right hand and raise the bump of the fracture from inside gently outward.
Your other hand, from outside the bone, governs its straightening. If the fracture of the jaw has split into two, it is necessary to use a splint on both sides until it can be straightened. If there has been growth in the teeth.
Or if it has separated, tighten what you desired from it to remain with gold or silver thread or a bristle, then place on the fractured jawbone a splint, and put on top of the splint a folded cloth, and put on top of the cloth a large tight bandage or a piece of leather sole equal in length to the jaw. Then tie it from above according to what seems fit for you to tie it and agrees with its joining so that it does not break, and order the patient to rest and silence, and make his food smooth porridge. If you think that something has changed in the shape in any way, then take care of it on the third day.
Then tie it from above according to what seems fit for you to tie it and agrees with its joining so that it does not break, and order the patient to rest and silence, and make his food smooth porridge. If you think that something has changed in the shape in any way, then take care of it on the third day. Then repair what he has compressed with mill dust and egg white or semolina flour after removing the splint from him, and put on the bandage a soft pad. As long as that bandage adheres to it and the bone has not changed its state, leave it.
Do not remove it until it heals and the fracture strengthens, for often this fracture strengthens in three weeks. If a hot swelling appears during that time, use what we have mentioned repeatedly to soothe it until the swelling goes away.
As for if the fracture is with a wound that you have seen, if a piece of bone or pieces have been exposed, be gentle in removing those splinters according to what is suitable for you to remove them with the instrument. If the mouth of the wound is narrow, widen it with the scalpel to the extent you need.
Then, after removing those splinters and nothing remains of them, mark the mouth of the wound if it is wide, otherwise, apply one of the ointments that are suitable for it. Cover the wound until it heals.
The fifth chapter.
In setting a broken collarbone.
The collarbone is most often broken from the front at the end of the shoulder, and it breaks in one of three ways: either it breaks and shatters into two pieces without splintering, which is the easiest to set.
Or that splinters are formed in the fracture, which is more difficult to set, or that the fracture occurs with a wound.  If the fracture is without a wound, then two assistants should be brought: one to hold the arm following the broken collarbone and the other to stretch the neck towards the opposite side.
Then set the fracture with your fingers until it takes on the proper shape and has no bumps or irregularities. If you need to extend it more, then place a ball of cloth or wool under the armpit of the afflicted person.
It should be positioned according to your need, extending and lifting the collarbone, and pressing the ball with your hand until the fracture is set properly. If you cannot pull the end of the collarbone outwards because it has sunk deep, then the afflicted person should lie on their back.
A medium pillow should be placed under their shoulder and an assistant should press down on their shoulder until the deeply embedded portion of the collarbone rises upwards. Then, correct the fracture and set it with your fingers.
If you feel that a splinter of the collarbone has broken off and is moving, then it should be incised and that splinter should be gently removed. If the splinter is lodged in the bone, try to cut it out with one of the instruments prepared for this purpose. After the instrument reaches below the collarbone, the instrument which preserves the peritoneum appears; it is an instrument of wood or iron and this is its shape.
It resembles a spoon without any carving and its width is according to what you need from the size of the bone, large or small. As for its length, it is also according to what you can do for work, and let it be two-sided as you see, one side wider and the other narrower.
If the wound which you made when extracting a piece of bone is wide and the hot tumor has been secured, bring together the edges of the wound with sutures. And if the opening is slight or you fear the hot tumor, close the wound with the rent and the rough materials according to the size of the cut.
If a hot tumor is present, moisten the rent with rosewater and vinegar or drink and put it on. As for tightening the bone if it is without a wound or cut, apply a compress made from flour dust mixed with egg white on the bone. And put the soft bandage on it, then put a ball under his armpit if necessary, then take a long turban whose width is an inch or so, then place a folded strip of cloth on the bandage and the compress, then take a splint from thin board. Its width is three fingers and so is its length, then insert it into a cloth bag, then insert that cloth bag with the splint into the place in the turban which is located on the site of the fracture, then tighten the turban on the fracture as you go around. And wrap it around his neck and under his healthy armpit and under the patient's armpit and turn it back and forth on each side, as you saw that the tightening holds the fracture tightly, which is not hidden from you.
And the whole point is that the splint does not come off the broken bone. Therefore, it is necessary to check the patient every day, and whenever you see the bandage relax and the splint has shifted, fix it and tighten the bandage. Then, make the patient sleep on his back and place a small pillow under his armpit when he sleeps at night.
So that his shoulder may be raised from his side and the fracture of the collarbone rise with the elevation of the shoulder, or bind his arm to his neck, and do not remove the bandage unless an accident occurs in the place of a boil or tumor for twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see reason for it. It returns to its state of tension and leave it until the fracture of the collarbone is set and becomes united, and intensifies and strengthens more during twenty-eight days. And perhaps in some people, it may be less than that.
The Sixth Chapter.
In setting a fracture of the shoulder.  It rarely breaks in the wide place, but rather its parts break. And when it breaks either from a position or in the middle, and this is known by touch. Then according to the shape of the fracture, set it.
Make it straight and return it to its natural shape as much as you can, then place on the place a powder of flour mixed with egg white and fine linen, put over it a piece of folded cloth, and place on top a wide bandage from wood.
Thin to the extent of the whole shoulder or slightly wider, and if there is a swelling under the bandage in one of the places of the shoulder, then straighten that swelling with fine linen until the bandage lies flat, then tie over it a long turban.
Tighten firmly and make sure of the bandage so that it does not move from its place, and inspect the bandage every day, for whenever the bandage relaxes, tighten it and straighten the bandage if it moves from its place, let the patient lie on his right side, and the shoulder will be set in twenty.
Days or twenty-five days, and if this count is completed, remove the bandage, and you are safe because it is one of the bones that does not fear its breaking or displacement, If a piece of bone protruded and was embedded under the skin, then cut it and remove it. Make what has been mentioned in the fracture of the collarbone for the treatment of the hot swelling, if anything of that happens.
Chapter Seven.
In setting a broken rib cage.
The chest may break in its middle, and this rarely occurs, but its edges are more likely to fracture and be shattered. As for its symptoms if it breaks in the middle, it leans downwards and is exposed to severe pain and shortness of breath and coughing.
And perhaps blood comes out, and the depression in the broken bone becomes apparent, not hidden to the touch. And its treatment is that the patient lies on his back and a cushion is placed between his shoulders, then his arms are pressed together and the ribs are gathered with the hands from both sides, and care is taken to straighten it on every side as much as possible.
And he continues until the bone returns to its proper shape, then a bandage is placed on it and a splint is put over it from a thin board of poplar or cypress or the like in light weight after wrapping it in cloth. And care is taken to tie it tightly on the broken bone so that it does not move, and the bandage is passed over to the back several times and tightened firmly, then the bandage is inspected at all times, and whenever it relaxes, it is tightened.
And if necessity demands to loosen it when a swelling appears in the place or pain or a tumor, then hasten to loosen it and remove the bandage and correct what has appeared from that by its treatment, then return the bandage if you see fit for that. And face and keep it tied until it heals.
Chapter Eight.
In setting a broken rib, know that the fracture occurs in the ribs in the thick places that follow the back and their edges from the front are exposed to harm because they are cartilaginous, and this is not hidden to the touch when examined with the fingers.
He sets it by bringing the fracture together with his fingers on the best-arranged side until the shape is equal to what is right and then he bandaged it and tightens the broken bone with a splint if need be. If the rib is fractured at an angle towards the inside, it causes the patient intense pain.
And a broken rib, as with the one exposed by a wound, because the bone pierces the diaphragm and also causes him difficulty breathing, coughing, and vomiting blood, this is hard to treat, and the ancients have devised many tricks for it.
Some of them said that it is necessary to make the food of the sick person what produces wind and air so that the belly inflates and expands and the fracture is driven outwards. We hate this lest it be a cause for the occurrence of inflammation if it has not occurred, and if it has occurred, then it increases in it and confirms it.
And some of them said that a poultice is placed on the place and then it is sucked forcefully, which is most like the method except that he fears that the poultice will be drawn excessively to the place due to its weakness, and some of them said it is necessary to cover the place with wool soaked in hot oil.
And it is made a cushion between the ribs until it fills, so that the band is flat if it is wrapped on a turn, and then the patient is treated with the treatment of the wound from food and medicine, if the patients condition is severe and unbearable. And the bone piercing the diaphragm was a painful breach, and we feared for the patient, so it is necessary to cut on the place and expose the broken rib, then we make under it the instrument that preserves the peritoneum which I have described before, and we cut the bone gently and remove it.
Then we gather the edges of the wound if it is large by stitching and treat it with ointments until it heals, if a hot tumor appears during that time, then hurry up and apply a poultice in rose butter on the place, and treat the patient with what soothes the tumor from inside also.
And he lies on his side which is lighter to him until he heals.
The Ninth Chapter.
In the setting of a fracture of the spine and neck.
But as for the bones of the neck if they are broken, which rarely happens to them and most often it is a contusion, and also the vertebrae of the back, also, if this happens to one of them and you want to know whether it heals or not. Look, then, and if you see that his hands have become stretched out and numbed and died, and he is not able to move them or stretch them or grasp them, and if you prick them or touch them with a needle, he does not feel it, nor does he find pain in them, know that it does not heal in most cases, for it is gone.
And if he moves them and feels them when pricked or touched, know that the marrow of the bone is safe, and that the patient will heal with treatment. If the fracture of the back occurs like this, and you want to learn whether it heals also or not.
Look at his legs, and if you see that they have become stretched out and what happened in the hands has happened to them, then if he lies on his back, air and feces come out without will, and if he lies on his stomach, urine comes out without will. And if he lies on his back and wants to urinate, he cannot do so, know that he is gone, and do not bother with his treatment. If none of this happens to him, the matter is less severe, and the treatment for what happened is to try to soothe the hot swelling.
By putting on the affected vertebra rose oil alone, or with roasted egg yolks, place it three times a day until the hot swelling subsides, then apply one of the strong, absorbent bandages to the spot and tighten it with a bandage, and order him to rest and be still.
He shall not sleep except on the side which does not cause him pain until he is healed. If a splinter or something else which has been broken off from the bone occurred at the time of injury, it is necessary to cut the skin and extract that bone. Then the edges of the wound are stitched together if it is large, and then treated with ointments until healed. If the last bone of the coccyx, which is the tailbone, is broken, it is necessary to insert the thumb of the left hand into the cavity.
And the broken bone is smoothed with the other hand as much as possible and as it can be straightened, then a bandage and splint are placed on it if necessary, and then tightened.
If you feel a splinter broken inside it, cut it open and remove it, and treat the wound with the treatment mentioned above until healed.
The Tenth Chapter.
In Setting a Broken Hip.
It is rare for the hip bones to break, but if they do break, it happens that the edges of the bone splinter and crack lengthwise and tilt inward, and this causes pain in the place, numbness, and paralysis of the leg on the side where the fracture occurred.
And to set it, you must pass your hand over it until you feel the fracture and see its shape. If the break is only at the edges, smooth that break according to what appears to you in straightening it until it resembles its natural shape.
If the break is lengthwise or if it has tilted inward, place the patient on his stomach until you are able to set the fracture. When you have straightened it, apply a bandage and then put a splint of wood or leather on top of it and tighten it firmly.
Do not fear for him that the fracture will shift or that the splint will come loose, and smooth out any bumps in the wraps until they fill it up until the tension takes hold on evenness.  Command the patient to sleep on his back or on his sound side; if he develops a hot swelling.
Release it and allow it to heal until the hot swelling subsides, then apply what soothes it as previously mentioned, then return to its dressing and tightening as appropriate. If splinters or fragments from its edges appear in the bone, it should not be removed.
Do not touch it but smooth it externally as we have said, and leave its tightening until it heals.
The Eleventh Chapter.
In setting a fractured humerus.
The humerus is that between the elbow and the shoulder, so if it breaks, set it on one of two sides: one being to take a rigid, smooth, medium-thickness splint like this image: .
And tie it at its ends with two cords, then hang it from a raised position and have the patient sit on a chair, then place his broken arm on the splint until his armpit adheres to the middle of the curve of the splint, then suspend something heavy above it.
Or, have a servant extend it downwards, then the physician smooths the fracture with his hands along with it until the fracture returns to what is appropriate, and the other side:  the patient lies on his back, and his hand hangs from his neck with a cord.
Then order two servants: one to hold above the fracture with his hands, and the other below it, extending each one in his direction, and if you want the extension to be stronger, tighten below the fracture with a cord and above it with a cord, and have each servant extend them in their direction.
So if the fracture is near the shoulder end, it is necessary that the middle of the cord be under the armpit and the other under the fracture toward the elbow, and similarly if the fracture is near the elbow, it is necessary that the cord be placed in that location and on the elbow itself.
Then the fracture is set gently without force until the fracture becomes level as it should and unites well, then it is bandaged if there is no hot swelling. If a hot swelling appears, leave off bandaging until the seventh day and place upon it a poultice moistened with vinegar and rose oil.
Until the swelling subsides, then bandage it. The method of bandaging is to place the bandage on the fracture, then place a new linen band over the bandage, then gather the arm on its own shoulder and place her hand open on her knee.
And carry the linen and bandage on the shoulder and arm so that the arm takes the place of splints if nothing prevents you from doing so, and nothing has changed in the broken bone. If you fear that something will change, then use splints.
And this is to place on the fracture itself a splint that is wider and stronger than all other splints, and let there be between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with an increase of three fingers from each side.
Then bandage the splints with the bandaging mentioned at the beginning of the treatise.
And this is that your bandaging on the site of the fracture be tighter.
And every time after the fracture, the bandaging is looser. If you see the placement of the splints and bandaging as we said at the time of your mending of the limb from that hour, then do so.
And if you fear the hot swelling, leave off the bandaging and splints until the seventh day as we have said, then inspect the bandage every three days so that no roughness or puffing occurs in the site, or the food is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive bandaging, then correct all of this as we have mentioned.
If you are confident that nothing of that sort will happen, then do not remove the bandage until after many days, and the patient should lie on his back with his hand on his stomach, and a cushion filled with wool should be placed under his shoulder.
Keep a watch at all times, day or night, so that the broken limb does not lose its shape or relax the ligament; repair it all with your effort. Make the food of the patient according to the order we have presented, so that the food is gentle first until the bone begins to harden.
When it is necessary to thicken his food, for the habit of the arm and leg is to harden in forty days; then he should bathe and be treated with ointments that are suitable for this, if the fracture is a gaping one that is festering.
Do not remove the splint from him until fifty days or two months.
The twelfth chapter.
In setting a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones and they are called the radius; one is small, which is the one following the thumb, and the other is large, which is located below the small one from below; perhaps the larger radius broke alone or the smaller one alone, and perhaps they both broke.
When the smaller radius above breaks, its mending is easier and its recovery faster; when the lower radius breaks, it was a bad fracture and its mending difficult and slower. And if both bones broke together, then if the bone that broke was the smaller radius above.
Then it is necessary for the physician, when mending it, to make its length easy and gently until he makes it straight; if the larger radius was broken, then it is necessary for him to make its length stronger. And if both radii were broken, then it is necessary for him to make the splint very strong.
It is necessary to place the shape of the hand when mending it and its length stretched out on a cushion, and the thumb of the hand should be above all the fingers, and the little finger below all the fingers, and the patient sitting upright on himself.
Let the pillow be at his side in height so that the patient does not labor with difficulty.
Then a servant extends the arm from below, either with his hand or with a bandage, and another servant extends it from above as well, then the physician sets the bone until he returns it to the best possible shape, if there are fragments of bone in the fracture, they are set.
Return every fragment to its place with all your effort, if a protruding fragment appears which is piercing the skin and you have no desire to force it, then cut through it and remove it in the manner mentioned previously, if the fracture was accompanied by a wound, a separate chapter has been allocated for it.
Then take its treatment from there, if a hot swelling appears in the first setting of the bone, apply a poultice made with qaytar, scented with rose oil and white wax, let it be medium between thick and thin and tighten the poultice on it gently until the swelling subsides.
Then remove the qaytar and place the prepared bandage from ground chalk with egg white, then carry the splints and let the splint placed on the fracture itself be slightly wider and stronger, and know that in most cases the number of arm splints will be six.
Whether the fracture was in one forearm or in both, then make your tightening on the site of the fracture stronger and tighter, and as you tighten upwards or downwards, make the tightening slightly looser, as mentioned previously at the beginning of the chapter.
Let the poultice that wraps around the fracture be a soft, moist one and not too hard, let the thread used to tighten it be from special cotton, medium between thin and thick as we described, and inspect the limb and the bandage after a few days.
If something happens that needs to be fixed, such as an irritation that occurs in the limb, it is necessary for the limb to be bathed with warm water until the irritation subsides, and the limb is left untightened at night until it rests, then tightening is resumed.
If the tension has relaxed and the bone has set, or something similar, then repair it to the best of your ability. Also look, if food is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tension, it is necessary to relax it a little and leave it for days until nourishment reaches it, then tighten it again.
If the patient does not experience any of what we have mentioned, then he should not be released until after twenty days or so. Then, suspend the patient's hand to his neck and let his arm be moderately extended. He should avoid strenuous movements and sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture of the arm heals in thirty days or in thirty-two, and perhaps in twenty-eight, all depending on the patient's constitution and strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
On Setting a Fracture of the Hand and Fingers.
The metacarpals of the hand and phalanges of the fingers rarely fracture, but they often experience contusion. So, when a fracture or contusion occurs in the hand...
...it is necessary for the patient to sit upright with a stool in front of him at an even height.
Then he places his hand on it, extended, and the bone servant stretches the broken bone and sets it straight until when it is joined well, then it is necessary to apply a bandage and splint if there is no hot swelling, then carry a plaster from above according to the place.
And I have included it in a soft cloth, if the fracture is downward toward the palm of the hand then make a shape like a ball from cloth and order the patient to grasp it with his broken hand then tighten it with a long cloth.
And let the plaster be from leather that is soft so that it touches the skin with the bending of all the hand and tighten it as necessary, if the fracture is outward then it is necessary to make a plaster above and another plaster below in the palm so that the hand remains open and standing.
Then he holds the tension as the hand turns and interlaces the fingers with the bandage, and if the fracture is to one of the phalanges of the fingers, if it is the thumb, let him make it smooth as befits then he tightens with the hand, and if you wish, make for him a small standing splint to set the fracture.
And it does not move, and if the fracture is to the rest of the fingers like the middle finger or the index finger or the ring finger or the little finger, let him make it smooth and bind it with the following healthy finger, or bind them all on allegiance, this is better, or place a small standing splint on them.
As we said in the thumb, and inspect while binding it, and after it from the hot swelling, oppose it with what is fitting when something of that happens, as what was repeated its description.
Chapter Fourteen.
In Setting a Fracture of the Femur.
The femur often breaks and it is felt because it moves forward and backward, and its setting is done by tightening a bandage above the fracture and another below the fracture, and the patient lies on his face.
Then he stretches each bandage servant to its side on moderation, this is if the fracture is in the middle of the bone, and as for if it is near the root of the femur, it is necessary that you tighten a soft bandage, either from wool or the like.
Near the groin so that the stretching falls above and the other bandage below the fracture, as well as if the fracture is near the knee, let the bandage be near the knee so that the stretching falls below, then the physician smooths the bone with both hands until he returns it to the example of the natural shape.
And the bone comes together well, then it is necessary to hold the bandage and tension if nothing happened to the limb, a hot swelling, if it happened to him, leave it for days until the hot swelling subsides then return to his treatment.
As for stretching it, it is necessary to wind a wide, stiff bandage around the fracture two or three times and leave what remains. Then the leg is stretched until the heel reaches the base of the thigh, and a long thread is inserted between the femur and tibia near the knee from below, and the ends of the thread are made above on both sides.
Then the remaining bandage is wound around the leg and thigh, then a splint is placed on the thigh in the same place as the fracture, and one splint is made on the tibia. Then the gap between the femur and tibia is filled with soft padding so that the tension is even.
Then begin pulling strongly from the middle at the site of the fracture three or four times, and as you move the bandage away from the fracture site, make your pull less and softer and looser.
Then go to the ends of the thread you inserted between the femur and tibia and tie them to the remaining splint from above, then pass the ends of the thread down until they reach the ankle, and pull on them also to tighten the edges of the splints on the other side.
So that the bandage does not come loose from its place, then leave the tension on it as long as the limb does not report pain or swelling or any such thing. If anything of that sort happens, act quickly to fix it and repair whatever has happened, as we have mentioned repeatedly.
If there is a fragment of bone, it is necessary to smooth it if you can, otherwise cut through it and remove it, and treat the wound with what has been mentioned previously until it heals. This fracture may be stretched from the femur without adding the leg with splints.
As we mentioned in the arm and shoulder, except that healing it this way does not expose the patient to limping. And if it is set alone without joining the leg to it, then the owner will inevitably limp always.
Know that the thigh strengthens in fifty days or a little more or a little less, all of that according to the difference in temperaments and other conditions.
The fifteenth chapter.
In the fracture of the kneecap.
The kneecap rarely suffers a fracture, but it often experiences contusions. If it fractures, it is either a split or broken into pieces, with or without a wound.
And you rely on this all by feeling, and set it by straightening what is scattered of its parts with your fingers until they come together and conform according to what the setting allows, gentleness, and perfection, then place the dressing and put a circular splint on top of it if that is needed.
And tighten the tension above that which corresponds to it, then take care of all the circumstances that we described in other fractures like the hot swelling and so forth by meeting each occurrence with what suits it until it heals.
The sixteenth chapter.
In setting the fracture of the leg.
The leg has two bones, one thick and called the femur, and the other thin and called the tibia. Both are subject to the types of fractures that occur in the arm bones. Therefore, its setting is like the setting of the arm, and the procedure is one and the same. If both bones fracture, the leg turns in all directions. If the thinner bone fractures, the leg turns forward. If the thicker bone fractures, especially from below, this should be clear to you.
So use the application of pressure and setting, and binding the splints accordingly, all except that if a leg fracture is a very bad fracture with many fragments, then the pressure should be less and lighter, and you should be gentle in its setting.
And in the leg, there is something extra from the arm's work: When you have finished setting the splints and all your work, take two strips of pine wood that are used for smoothing the rooms between the cracks of the boards or are made from palm fronds or similar.
Choose from them what has a little thickness and is not thin, and let its length be on the length of the leg from the knee down, then wrap around each one two layers of cloth along its length, placing one on the leg and the other on the opposite side.
And let it be from the knee to the bottom of the foot, then tie the two fasces in three places from the ends and the middle, for with this bridle the leg prevents leaning right and left, and is well tightened, and a gutter of wood may be used along the leg.
And it is placed in it to preserve it from movement, and most of the time it is necessary to do this in a fracture if it is accompanied by a special wound, then inspect the leg every day and pay great attention to it for swelling or puffiness or anything else. Then when something of that happens, meet it with what is appropriate until it heals, and the bone of the leg sets in about thirty days or so.
The seventeenth chapter.
In a fracture of the bones of the leg and fingers.
As for the heel, it does not expose to a fracture of the bone, but as for the bones of the leg, they may be exposed to a fracture, and the fingers also rarely experience a fracture, but rather injury in most cases.
So if a fracture of the leg bones occurred and you saw that some of those bones protruded over each other, let the patient put his foot on the ground upright like walking, then you place your foot on what rose from those bones and press it down and level it.
Until it returns to its places, then carry the bandage and splint from above, and put under the sole of the foot a small board with two flat heads, then tighten the foot tightly after wrapping it with cloth and everything else you need.
If three or four days have passed, release the bandage, and you will find that the bones are level, whether they were broken or dislocated, so act as you see fit. As for if some of the fingers are broken, force them and level them according to what I described to you for setting the fingers of the hand.
Then make a splint for the broken finger, along the length of the finger and let it be slightly narrower than it. Then place under this foot the board which I described to you and tighten it firmly. If what broke was two or three or more bones
Make on each finger a splint the width of a  strip in soft cloth and tighten the board at the bottom of the foot, let each head of the heads of the board be outside the belly of the foot to hold it well.
And you should not forget to take heed of all that we have mentioned to you in all fractures from the conditions which we have mentioned and meet each condition with what is necessary.
Chapter Eighteen.
In the fracture of a woman's pubis bone and the man's.
When the woman's pubis bone is fractured, have her sit upright then slightly bend her back and hold her from behind and the midwife packs her vagina with cotton until it fills it and becomes in her vagina like a ball.
Then the woman is shaken and her pelvis is raised slightly, then that cotton comes out until it becomes at the entrance of the vagina like a ball and the bone fracture returns. Then she lays a cushion on her back, and when she wants to urinate, she removes the cotton gently until she urinates.
And it is returned in the way it was first inserted, then she returns to her cushion. Do this for seven days or so, as it will heal. If you wish to take a sheep's bladder, tighten its mouth with a short tube and insert the whole bladder into her vagina.
Then inflate the tube with force until the bladder inflates inside the vagina, as the fracture returns. Then pack it with cotton and stay on what we have described for days until it heals. As for when the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, it is used in its healing.
Its treatment is as we have described for the hip bone, and it is not hidden from you that what is right in these strange fractures which rarely occur, especially for those who have some experience and understand this book well, because the fracture itself indicates to you in most cases.
On the right path in its healing and stretching, so understand.  But as for the case of a man if his thigh bone fractures at the hip joint, he puts a poultice into it, then places a bandage of linen on top, and binds it, leaving it for three days or thereabouts until it heals.
The Nineteenth Chapter.
In setting fractures of the bones if they are with a wound.
It is necessary for one who presents with a fracture along with a wound, and especially if the bone is large like the femur or humerus or similar, to act quickly and separate it from its time if the conditions of trepanation have aided you as we presented previously.
If the wound is bleeding, it is necessary to act quickly and cut it by applying powdered saffron to it; if nothing else is available, then take to setting the fracture on that very day and do not delay it.
If a hot tumor does not occur, if a hot tumor occurs, leave its setting until the ninth day until the hot tumor subsides and do not approach it at all on the third and fourth days because it is exposed to bad symptoms.
If the broken bone is exposed to the skin, it is necessary that you gently push it back and set it with your hands with a slight stretch, and if you cannot push it back and set it with your hands, then set it with this instrument which is made of iron, seven or eight finger lengths long. And expose it to the extent of the wound.
And therefore, it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of them, according to what he needs for treatment in each type of fracture, and let them be round with a slight thickness so that they do not bend when pressed upon them during work.
It is sharply pointed at its end and has a bend at its tip, with its upper part being thicker and from half of it downwards becoming very thin. This is its image.
It is called in Greek "perim," they want a small awl, so it should be that its sharp point which is bent, should be on the end of the bone coming out and push it with it once until when the bone returns and becomes some level then set the ends of the fracture to each other.
If the broken end is slender and the instrument did not grasp it well, then it is necessary to cut the end of that bone until the instrument can take hold of it, if it could not return the bone as we described at all, then cut it with pieces similar to those we mentioned.
Or spread it with one of the saws as much as you can, then remove what remains in the bone of roughness and thin peels, if you returned the bone and found the injured person after its return severe and annoying pain, know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
If you were able to return it to its natural place, then do so because you benefit the injured person a great benefit, if you complete your bone setting, dip a cloth in a black astringent syrup especially if you are in summer and do not put on the wound a poultice or anything greasy in it.
Lest it becomes rotten and corrupt, then use splints while you are free from setting the bone and leave the wound uncovered by cutting with scissors in the bandages a hole according to the size of the wound.
And beware all caution that you tighten the wound with the fracture because many times ignorant physicians did that and they caused to their patients either death or gangrene or abscesses, let your tightening be lenient relaxed unlike the tightening of other fractures.
If the wound is bad or was a large wound and you feared on it some of the bad symptoms we described and he found pain in the place disturbing, then it is not necessary to put splints on it and make for him bandages from strong cloth in place of the splints and tighten them with it.
If it is after a day or two and you see the wound has begun to produce pus, remove the cloth that was placed on it with the drink, then use the poultices and ointments which we are accustomed to using for wounds, such as the four-part ointment and similar ones.  It is also necessary for you to apply a bandage.
And inspect the wound every day in the evening and morning until it closes and heals, and it is necessary to place the limb in a position so that pus can easily flow downwards. If the wound has passed many days and it has not closed or the pus has not stopped flowing, know that there are small bone splinters within it.
It is necessary to inspect the wound with a probe, and whatever of those splinters are clean, remove them and take them out. Whatever of them are not clean and are causing pain to the limb, then cut them and remove them as best you can.
If a cold, sore, or another type of corruption and ulceration appears in the wound, it is necessary to treat each one with its appropriate remedy as described in its chapter.
And of what it is necessary to pay attention to and keep in mind if a large bone breaks and the limb is bent like the femur, humerus and similar major limbs, do not attempt to pull it or remove it. For often, that exposes one to death. Leave it until it decays, and perhaps it will fall out on its own after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see a place for treatment, otherwise leave it.
Chapter Twenty.
On Treating the Complication That Occurs After Some Fractures.
This complication often occurs after the healing of a fracture, especially those near the joints. It makes the limb unsightly and may prevent it from performing its natural function.
If the abscess is soft, employ astringent medications such as frankincense, myrrh, aloes, and gum Arabic, or similar substances, by taking some or all of them and mixing them with a styptic liquid, egg white, or vinegar.
And carry it to the abscess in a compress and bind it tightly, keeping the pressure on for many days, then remove it and reapply another until the abscess disappears, God willing, or you can put a compact lead plate on top of it.
For lead has the property of dissolving everything within the body. If the abscess becomes hard, severe, and urgent to be removed, then cut into it from its top and remove the pus that comes out or clear it with some kind of tool until it goes away, then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In treating a fracture, if it sets and the limb remains soft after that, unlike its original nature.
If a bone breaks and the limb remains soft and weak afterward, it is due to many reasons. One of these reasons is either because of excessive loosening and improper tying of ligaments, or because of excessive tightening of the ligaments so that nourishment cannot reach the limb, or because of excessive rubbing, or excessive movement at the wrong time, or because of a deficiency of blood in the patient's body and his weakness.
And the treatment for this is to nourish the patient and enrich his body until his blood increases, and using baths and bringing him joy and happiness, and so on, then place a poultice on the limb to attract nourishment to it or keep it rubbing with warm water. Until nourishment flows in it and it returns to its natural form.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
In treating fractured bones if it is splinted crookedly and its function is restricted improperly.
When a member appears after healing to have deformity or outgrowth of the fractured bone or complexity and ugliness for that reason, a picture of the member is shown. Except that the limb did not prevent its natural function, it is not fitting to accept the saying of one who claims to break the limb from the top and many ignorant physicians and surgeons used to do this in our land.
And this act is highly blameworthy, it leads to great harm and deformity. But if the deformity and complexity are mild, then it should be treated with water that has been boiled with soft herbs such as rue, its root, crown imperial, and the like.
And it is bandaged with soft dressings such as diachylon well made, or the sap of rue is taken and beaten with chicken fat, sheep fat, and it is bandaged with it, or ripe figs are taken and pounded with pigeon droppings and similar medicines called anti-calcification.
And gradually the complexity is dissolved by continuous gentle massage with the hands and the movement of the limb in all directions at all times. If the deformity has become prominent, severe, hardened, and necessity calls for its treatment with iron.
Then it is necessary to cut above it and release the connection of the bone, and cut what remains of the deformity or bone with delicate cuts, and use gentleness and care in doing so, then treat the wound as mentioned before until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
The jaw is the displacement of a joint from its place, so it hinders movement and disfigures the shape of the limb, and causes great pain and suffering to the patient. So when a case of dislocation of the jaw appears, it is necessary to immediately attend to its reduction.
Do not delay treating it, for if you do, the swelling of the place will increase and make it difficult to reset the jaw. Therefore, it is not advisable to delay treatment or move or stretch it while it is swollen because it often causes pain and severe discomfort in the patient.
But if this happens, it is necessary to quickly treat the patient with a bloodletting and then leave it until the swelling subsides a little, then massage the area with warm water and oil, and then gently reset it and treat each part according to what will be mentioned in its place.
I have also arranged the sections on the jaw according to what precedes in the fracture, from the upper part of the body to the lower.
The Twenty-Fourth Chapter.
On Treating the Lower Jawbone Fracture.
The separation of the jawbones occurs rarely except in a situation where it comes loose on either face, either slightly moving and relaxing somewhat or completely dislocating so that they are completely relaxed. It may be to the extent of the chest so that saliva flows from the patient and he cannot hold it back, nor can he withstand applying his jaw and mumbles when speaking.  However, if the dislocation is slight, it may return in most cases by itself with the easiest thing.
But if the dislocation is complete, it is necessary to immediately attend to its return and not delay it at all. This is done by a servant holding the patient's head and the physician inserting his thumb into the base of the jaw inside his mouth if the jaw is on one side.
Or both thumbs if the jaw is on both sides and the rest of his fingers from the outside adjusts them and orders the patient to relax his jaw and release it to go in all directions, and the physician adjusts the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
If it is difficult to return them, especially if all the bones are broken, use a compress with hot water and oil until they are easy to put back in place and do not delay their return at all as we have said.  When they return and settle and the patient's mouth closes and does not relax, then at that time place on them pads of cloth along with plugs made from wax and oil.
Then it is gently tied with a loose bandage, and the patient sleeps on his back with his head elevated between two pillows so that he does not move his limbs or try to chew anything but rather eats soft food.
Until the pain goes away and the jaw is united, let him eat what he wishes, and let him do so gently and not force his mouth open when eating, drinking, or yawning until the jaw heals completely.
If it is difficult to return the jaws if they have broken apart at some time and have not returned to their places, then often there are fevers and constant headaches and perhaps the patient's belly swells and he vomits repeatedly. If you see this, know that it is defective.
And many die from this within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On Setting the Collarbone and Shoulder End.
As for the collarbone, it does not break from the inside due to its connection with the chest, but rather breaks outwards and this is apparent to the touch. To set it, the patient lies on his back and extends his arms.
Then you press the location with your hand, pressing forcefully, and it returns. Then a bandage and pads are placed on it and tightened. As for its end that is next to the shoulder and connected to it, this does not dislocate except rarely. If it dislocates one day, then it should be returned and set as mentioned before and as you deem appropriate.
Then you place upon it the dressing, the padding, and the tightening, and order the sick person to keep to rest and quiet until he heals, and with this treatment alone the dislocated shoulder is set back in its place as well.
The Twenty-Sixth Chapter.
On Setting a Dislocated Shoulder.
Know that the shoulder dislocates in three directions: one is a dislocation toward the armpit and downwards, the second is a dislocation towards the chest, and perhaps it dislocates upwards towards the upper shoulder, which is rare.
And it does not dislocate backwards towards the shoulder blade nor forwards towards the nerve, and most often it dislocates and exits downwards towards the armpit, especially in those whose flesh is scant because it exits quickly in them and enters quickly.
As for those whose flesh is abundant, it is the opposite, meaning that it exits with difficulty and enters with difficulty, and perhaps a blow or a fall occurs to some people, causing swelling of the shoulder and a hot abscess, so they think that it has dislocated, so one must examine it until you ascertain its health.
Then, when examining it, you progress in its treatment, and the dislocation is recognized if it is downwards towards the armpit; if you press between the dislocated shoulder and the healthy one, you find a visible difference, and you find the head of the shoulder twisted, and under the armpit upon touching the head of the shoulder as if it were an egg.
And the sick person is unable to raise his hand to his ear nor move it in all movements, and similarly if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards, you find that clearly visible upon touch, not concealed.
And this dislocation may be easily set if it is fresh or the sick person is a boy, and to set it, have a servant raise his hand upwards, then you place your thumbs under his armpit and lift the joint forcefully upwards to its place, and the servant raises his hand and stretches it upwards.
Then he cuts it downwards, for it returns quickly. If it does not return as we mentioned, and if the jaw has been fractured for many days, then it is necessary that the patient bathe in hot water and use a poultice which relaxes and softens like boiling the roots of  black cumin, aniseed, and crown imperial in water, and he should apply this.
Then the patient lies on his back and a ball of wool is placed under his armpit, which should be moderate between softness and firmness. Then the physician places his heel on the ball and grasps the patient's hand with his own and extends it downwards.
And he pushes the ball with his leg, for it returns to its place. If you wish, return it in this way: which is to bring a man taller than the patient and make him stand beside him from the side, and insert his knee under the armpit of the patient and raise his armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air. And another servant pulls the patient's hand downwards towards his belly. If the patient is light, it is necessary to hang something else from him to weigh him down, for it returns the jaw within an hour. It can also be returned in another way.
And this is by placing in the ground a long plank whose head is round like the pestle of a mortar, neither thick nor thin, and it is placed under the armpit of the patient after putting a soft cloth on top of the plank, and the patient stands on the length of the plank.
Then he extends his hand downwards from the other side, and he also extends his body from the other side with force, for the joint returns to its place quickly. If it is difficult to return it by all that has been mentioned, use this treatment: which is to take a plank about two arms long. And its width is the length of four fingers and its thickness is the length of two fingers, it should have a round head to make it easy to enter into the depth of the armpit in this way: .
Then a soft, flexible band is tied around the round head so as not to injure the sick person's wood. Then it is placed under the armpit and the whole hand and arm are extended onto the wood and downwards, and the wood is tied to the upper arm and forearm and hand.
Then the arm is placed on a beam. The body is supported across and the hand extended downwards, leaving the rest of the body hanging from the other side, because the joint enters in its time. When it has entered fully, in any way possible, then under the armpit a ball of moderate size of wool should be placed.
Then the prepared dressing of flour dust with frankincense and egg white is applied to the whole shoulder as it rotates above, then the ball under the armpit is pulled tightly and rotated on the dressing from above, and his hand is suspended up to his neck.
And he leaves his hand unmoved for seven days, and it is necessary to make the food of the sick person little until the limb strengthens, because this is faster for healing, then after seven days or five it is treated with movement. If it hardens and does not relax, it has healed.
If the joint frequently dislocates due to dampness or another ailment, it is necessary to use cauterization with the three types of cautery as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization. If all this has been done and the bandage was removed after seven days and the joint did not set.
The dressing was reapplied and tightened several times, but it did not set and fell off, and relaxed, and could not be raised above. Know then that the nerve in the shoulder head has been severed or stretched or relaxed, know at that time that the joint will never stay in its place ever again.
As for the jaw which is near the chest and breast, and behind its individual part, it is treated by pushing and stretching with the hands until it returns, and all tightening and treatment are applied to it until it heals. If after healing, stiffness appears in the limb and movement is slower.
Let the sick person use the bath frequently until that joint softens and returns to its original nature.
The Twenty-Seventh Chapter.
In Treating a Dislocated Shoulder.
The shoulder joint is difficult to dislocate, and it is also difficult to reduce; it can be dislocated in all directions, especially forward or backward. Its dislocation is not hidden from you because it is visible under the eye and to the touch, no matter what shape it has been dislocated into.
And if you compare the dislocated shoulder with a healthy one, it becomes clear to you visually by examining its dislocation; the arm cannot be bent, nor can you touch his elbow with it. It is necessary that you act quickly and reduce the dislocation from the moment it happens before a hot tumor develops in it.
Because if it develops a hot tumor, its reduction becomes difficult and it may not heal at all, especially if the dislocation is backward; because it is the most difficult of all types of dislocations and the most painful, and often death accompanies it.
And if its cure is possible, that which can be reduced: let a servant extend his hand with both hands and the arm outstretched, and the physician's hand on top of the shoulder joint, from underneath it, and he pushes the joint with his thumbs or the base of his palm until it returns to its place.
As for if the dislocation is forward, it may be reduced by bending the hand once until it hits with the base of its palm the elbow which confronts it. If the dislocation does not return, then use strong and forceful pulling; that is, let two servants extend the arm.
And the sick person, too, shall be grasped by two servants so that it does not leave during the pulling; then the arm is turned in every direction after being wrapped on his hand with a folded cloth or a wide piece of fabric. And if the physician starts with his hands to massage the joint with oil, it will become easier for the joint to slip.
Then, he pushes the joint forcefully until it returns to its place. And after its return, a bandage containing compression and drying with egg white should be applied to it and tightened tightly. The arm is suspended from the neck of the patient and left for days.
Then, if the joint has set in its place, release the bandage from it and leave it. But if you see that the joint is not firmly fixed, reapply the bandage and splint and leave it for days until it becomes firm, then release it.  If a swelling occurs after the return of the joint and its release.
If movement is difficult, he should apply a warm bath and gentle massage and rubbing until it softens or place a thick layer of fat from a sheep on the joint, then bind it and leave it for a day and night, then remove it and take him to the bath. If he sweats, rub the joint with moderate friction.
Then, apply the fat again a second and third time with bathing until it softens. And if you wish to place a layer of moistened cow's dung with butter on it and bind it tightly, do so several times because it will soften and return to its original state.
The Twenty-Eighth Chapter.
In the Treatment of a Broken Wrist.
The wrist joint often breaks, and its repair is easier than other joints except that it should be quickly set the moment it breaks before a swelling occurs or a hot swelling develops in the area.
To set it, place the injured arm on a board and extend its hand. The physician places his palm on the top of the joint and pushes it until it returns, but he should examine to see if the fracture has moved inward towards the inside of the hand.
If the fracture has moved outward toward the outside of the hand, then the patient should place the palm of his hand on the board when extending and setting it. But if the fracture is protruding towards the back of the hand, then he should place the palm of his hand on the board so that the physician's hand falls on the same top of the joint. If it returns to its original position immediately, but not, tightly bind it with a bandage to reduce swelling.
Leave him and do not disturb him, for he cannot bear it, nor can you recover from it after the days have passed except that the joint remains on its surface and does not harm the patient except if the hand stretches and is unable to grasp anything.
Then you will know that the nerve has been severed or bruised, and there is no remedy except to tighten it with a sling, perhaps it may be of benefit and perhaps it may not be of any use at all. When the radius has been set, apply the bandage we have described, then tie it and leave it for five days.
Then it is removed and the hand is exercised, if its movement becomes difficult and there appears something of the flesh on its surface, soften it with warm water and rub it repeatedly until it softens.
The Twenty-Ninth Chapter.
In the Treatment of Fractures of the Fingers.
Fingers may be fractured in every direction, so when a finger is fractured to the outside or inside of the palm, extend the finger and push the fracture with your thumb until it returns, then tie the head of the finger and suspend it towards the direction it was fractured.
Leave it for two days then release it and extend it until it becomes straight on that day, so if it is at night you tie it in the same way, do not stop releasing it during the day and exercise it with movement and tie it at night, do this for days until it strengthens.
And so you do if it is fractured to the inside of the hand, tie it towards the same direction and do with it what you did in the first instance until it heals, and so do with it when it is fractured in any other direction.
The Thirtieth Chapter.
In the Treatment of Fractures of the Spine.
When a fracture occurs to one of the vertebrae of the back or neck, either completely fractured or many vertebrae have moved from their places there is no treatment for it because death quickly comes upon the patient.
A sign of this is that the stool of the sick person comes out involuntarily, and he cannot hold it back, and many times some of his limbs relax, whether his legs or arms or one of them. And if one bead (of sweat) moves from its place, it often moves.
And it moves to four directions, the one that moves to the back is called a hump, and as for its treatment, if you look at it and find that the hump occurred from childhood, there is no treatment for it, nor is there any cure from it whatsoever. But the one that happened from a fall or a blow and the like...
For the ancients mentioned it with various treatments in a long discourse, most of which do not return much benefit. I have abridged from that what is sufficient to replace much of what they said, approaching the meaning, explaining it, and depicting the instrument differently than they described and explained it.
So I say that the hump that occurs in the chest from the front has no remedy or cure for it, and the same is true for the one on both sides. But only the one that occurs on the back is treated, specifically with what I will describe, which is to stretch the patient on his face.
On a flat counter near a wall, and spread a damp cloth under him so as not to hurt his chest, then place a standing plank embedded in a pit in the ground at one end of the counter towards his head and another plank towards his feet at the other end of the counter.
And a servant holds the plank, so that it is not fixed in the pit, and another servant holds the other one in that position, then wrap around the patient's chest and under his armpit with a soft and tight cloth, and stretch one end of the cloth to the plank at his head and tie it there.
Then tighten with another cloth over his hips and thighs and above his ankles, then gather all the ties and tie them to the other plank at his feet, and each servant stretches his plank towards his side with the tie, and the two planks do not move from their original positions.
Unless they are secured as we mentioned, the physician places his hands on the bead with force until it returns, or he places a board on it and leans on the board with his legs until it returns. If it does not return with this treatment, take a board that is about three cubits long.
And you dig in the wall which we said should be near the patient, a place where the end of the board enters, then you place the middle of the board on the hump, and the physician puts his legs on the other end and pulls tightly until he compresses the bead.
And it returns to its place, and if you wish to make it with the screw that is tightened by hand, which is to dig into the ground at the head of the patient, in the end of the room, two pieces of wood which are a cubit long, and at his feet in the end of the room also two pieces of wood.
And let there be after each piece of wood a span, and it was made in each piece of wood a hole where the screw runs, and the wood is all secured in the ground firmly so that it does not move at all, and you insert a round rod which is the screw that is wound in the holes of the two pieces of wood.
And in its end there is a hole which was secured with a rod a span long which is wound, and in the other two pieces of wood like that, then you tighten the rope which you tightened on the chest of the patient in the screw at his head and the ropes that you tightened on his legs in the screw at his feet.
Then a servant stands at each screw tightening his hand with the wrench that winds it, and the physician levels the hump as we presented before, and this is a picture of the screw and the bed and the patient:
Then after the hernia returns and the place is leveled, it is necessary to apply the dried bandage with egg white then the plaster, and then you place on top of the bandage a splint from a board which is three fingers wide or so and its length is as much as takes the place of the hump.
And upon some of the sound beads, then it is bound with the appropriate bandage and the patient uses fine nutritious food until it heals. If some protrusion remains in the place at the end of healing, it is necessary to use medicines that soften and relax along with using the plate which we described a long time ago.
And a lead plate may be used in that. And a protrusion may appear at the end of the back beads, and it is thought to be a slipping (of something), but it is an extra bone that has protruded, so do not expose it to this treatment, for perhaps death may occur.
Chapter thirty-one.
In the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the elbow joint are only subject to dislocation, and they are not subject to what other joints are subjected to of slight displacement and dislocation. And the hip joint dislocates in four directions.
And that is because it dislocates inward, outward, forward, and backward, and most of the time it dislocates inward, and rarely does it dislocate forward or backward, and a sign of its dislocation inward is that if you align the limb of the healthy patient with the diseased one, it will be longer.
And the knee will be more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend his leg at the hip, and the place below the hip will be warm and soft as if the head of the thigh has gone there.
And a sign of the one who has dislocation outward is that his symptoms are opposite to these symptoms, and a sign of the one who has dislocation forward is that he extends his leg fully except that he cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and even if he wants to walk forward, he cannot.
And he is obstructed in his urination and feels pain in his hip, and when he walks, his weight is on the heel, and a sign of the one who has dislocation backward is that he cannot straighten his knee and cannot bend it before bending his hip, and his leg is also shorter than the other.
The thigh should be relaxed and the head of the femur at the level of the vertebra, as for the return of this fracture, it should be examined if the fracture has become old and remained with its owner without an attempt to correct it, and remained in its state, then there is no treatment at all.
So it should not be subjected to manipulation, as for the one whose fracture is recent and whatever type it may be of the four types of fractures, hasten to make the joint bend and extend it inside and outside and move it right and left so perhaps.
It returned and did not need anything else from treatment, if it does not return like this then it is necessary that you prepare a strong assistant to extend his leg from below either with his hands or with a rope tied on his leg above the knee and another assistant extends it from above.
So that he puts his hands under his armpit and then pulls with a soft rope at the root of the thigh and holds one end of the rope, another assistant third is needed and his extension is either from the front from the side of the buttock to the collarbone or from behind to the back.
And they should be extended all at once until the patient rises with his body from the ground and remains hanging, for this type of extension is a common type for the four types, if the fracture returns as we have said, otherwise there is a need for each type of what I mentioned from special treatment.
As for its special return if the fracture is inwards, it is necessary that the patient lie on his right side and then a rope is placed on the root of the thigh between the head of the femur and the place under the buttock.
Then the rope is extended upwards from the side of the buttock to the top of the body to the collarbone, and then another strong assistant takes hold with his arms around the thick part of the thigh of the patient and extends it outwards with a strong pull as it returns to its place.
This type is easier than all other types of treatment by which this member is returned.  If it proves difficult for you and does not respond to this type of treatment at all, then it is necessary that you bind both legs of the sick person firmly with a strong, soft bandage on the heels and on the knees.
And it should be after each one of its owner the measure of four fingers, and the injured leg is extended more than the other by the measure of a finger, then the sick person is hung from the head with wood that is in the house and it should be from the ground the measure of an arm.
Then you command a strong boy to embrace the thigh and hang the sick person another boy and push the other boy embracing the thigh with force, for the joint returns to its place quickly, as for his special return if the bone is outside.
Then it is necessary that the sick person lie on the board as we described in the owner of the hump and tighten the bandage on his injured leg especially and on his chest, then two planks are placed one at his feet and the other at his head, then an extra plank is placed in the middle of the board firmly attached.
A wet cloth may be wrapped over it so as not to harm the sick person, and the plank should be between his thighs so that he does not fall down when pulled, then each servant stretches to his side and the doctor smooths the hip with his hands, if it responds to returning.
And if not, then put the plank on him and press it as we mentioned in the hump except that it is necessary that the sick person lie on his right side, and if the dislocation is to the front, then it is necessary that you stretch the leg of the injured person once while he is in this description exactly on the board.
And the doctor places the palm of his right hand on the injured collarbone and presses it with his other hand, and at the same time he makes the pressure extended downwards towards the knee, and if the dislocation is to the back, it is not necessary that the injured person be stretched downwards.
It is elevated above the ground, but it should be placed on a solid thing, as it should also be from its hip and knee to the outside, according to what we mentioned about its position on the bench, while lying on its face and the bandages are tightened.
As we mentioned before, it is necessary to use the clamp with the board also on the place where the joint exited, so that if the return of the hip joint is complete as it should be, and the sign of its return is not hidden from you, which is that you extend the leg of the patient.
If you see them level and the patient flexes and extends his leg without difficulty, know that the member has returned as it should. Then, align the thighs and apply a bandage, tightening with a turban so tightly that the hip does not move in any direction.
And the patient must be confined to bed for three or four days, then remove the bandage and compare the leg with the other. If you see them equal in length, know that the fracture has set, so release the patient to walk. But if you see any looseness in it...
Go back and bandage him and tighten it according to your first tightening, and leave him also for three days, then remove it and walk slowly on it for a few days until it is strong by God's will.
The Second Thirty Chapter
In the Treatment of a Knee Fracture.
The knee breaks in three ways: inward, outward, and downward (meaning backward), and it does not break forward from the bottom. And the sign of its fracture is to order the patient to press his leg against his thigh.
If it does not stick to the thigh, know that the knee is fractured. And the setting of all types of fractures is that the patient sits down with his legs extended if he is able, and a helper sits behind him to grasp his waist and pull him back slightly, then you sit on his thighs.
